independence referendum**]{.mark}, should he gain office]{.underline}.
That said, while **[[polls currently favor him]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
much can happen between now and Election Day and his victory is hardly
assured.

Lots of History Here

The issue of Quebec and its relationship with English-speaking Canada
has been fraught ever since the colony of New France was ceded to
Britain in 1763 under the Treaty of Paris which ended the Seven Years'
War. Both under British rule and after the creation of Canada as a
self-governing dominion in 1867, the role of the French language and the
civil law system in Quebec was maintained.

Through the nineteenth and first half of the twentieth century, Quebec
remained a relatively inward-looking society, dominated by local
French-speaking political elites, an English-speaking Montreal-based
business class, and the Catholic Church, which had a tight grip on
education and social norms.

However, by the 1960s, Quebec had broken away from its long stagnation
and had become an increasingly urbanized, educated, and secular society.
The province underwent the so-called "Quiet Revolution" in which its
leaders pressed forward with significant modernization reforms,
including the nationalization of the power generation sector and the
construction of huge hydro-electric dams, the expansion of the
university system, and the creation of extensive state pension and
medical insurance systems.

This was coupled with the emergence of a new, self-confident,
French-speaking business class.

While the leaders who sponsored these developments did so within the
framework of Canada's existing confederation, the idea that the people
of Quebec should be "maîtres chez nous"---masters in our own
home---began to evolve into demands for outright independence.

The independence movement had a violent far-left fringe in the "Front de
Libération du Québec" (FLQ), which took its inspiration from such
sources as the Algerian War of Independence against France and the Cuban
Revolution.

The FLQ's efforts as an urban guerrilla movement began with bombing
attacks against government and business installations and culminated in
the 1970 kidnapping of a British commercial diplomat and the provincial
minister of labor, who was ultimately killed by the group. These acts of
terrorism, however, proved to be the FLQ's undoing as the federal
government, led by Pierre Trudeau, moved decisively against it, breaking
up its cells and controversially putting troops onto the streets of
Montreal.

While the FLQ's violent extremism was rejected by Quebec's public, the
Parti Québécois (PQ), which combined commitment to independence with an
affinity for European-style social democracy, had real success, becoming
a major political force. In 1976, it gained control of Quebec's National
Assembly, and its leader, a longtime sovereignty advocate René Lévesque,
served as premier through 1985.

Following this, the PQ went into opposition. As federalist leaders
governed Quebec, it returned to power under three different leaders from
1994 to 2003 and again from 2012 to 2014. Thereafter, it lost impetus,
at least until recently.

Despite Quebec voters having voted the PQ into office at different
times, they have rejected independence on the two occasions in which it
was put to them in referenda. The first time, in 1980, it was decisively
defeated, 59.56 percent to 40.44 percent. It was defeated again in 1995,
although this time by a hair's breadth margin, 50.58 percent to 49.42
percent.

Those who currently see independence as unlikely note that support for
it has been stagnant for years at around thirty-five percent and that
younger voters do not seem enthused by the prospect.

Back to the Future

However, opinion leaders in English-speaking [Canada]{.underline} may be
beginning to fear that they [will have to **confront the issue** of
Quebec separatism anew]{.underline}. This concern has been laid out most
extensively in an October 2024 article by Gerald Butts in The Walrus, a
Canadian journal of opinion. Butts served as Justin Trudeau's principal
advisor from 2015 to 2019 and is now vice chairman of the Eurasia Group,
a New York-based geopolitical risk consultancy.

Butts is aware that [[independence **currently** lacks]{.mark} majority
[support]{.mark} within Quebec.]{.underline}

**[[However]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [[he makes the analogy to]{.mark}
the referendum in which **[British voters]{.mark}** favored **[leaving
the E]{.mark}**uropean **[U]{.mark}**nion]{.underline}, where "(T)he
**[['Remain']{.underline}]{.mark}** side **[[was swept
away]{.underline}]{.mark}**... by a colorful, boisterous" 'Leave' team.
Butts worries that, even though, in his view, Canada is one of the
world's great success stories, ["(a)s was the case with Britain in the
run-up to **Brexit**, Canada's mood is unrelentingly grim]{.underline}."

He fears that as a result, [[**unlike** in]{.mark} 19[95]{.mark}, where
there was a massive grassroots effort by English-speaking Canadians to
persuade Quebec voters that their presence in Canada was greatly valued,
[a]{.mark} sour "**[let them go]{.mark}**" [tone **might
prevail**]{.mark}]{.underline}, aggravated by "angry tirades... \[by\]
keyboard warriors." Butts also suggests that [**[Russian
disinfo]{.mark}**rmation operations [may have]{.mark} had [a]{.mark}
[**role** in Brexit and could have one in]{.mark} the lead-up to a
referendum in [Quebec]{.mark}.]{.underline}

However, what is certain is that within Canada, a PQ victory would put
independence back at the center of national debate, making it all the
more difficult for the country to deal with the range of other issues it
faces, such as fixing its persistently sluggish economic growth and low
productivity, balancing its commitment to environmental protection with
the importance of its energy and mining sectors, and maintaining its
place in a world in which great power competition is making
international relations ever more complex.

Lots to Untangle

[An actual victory for independence]{.underline} in a referendum [would
provoke enormous constitutional, economic, and political
issues]{.underline}. First of all, in response to a ruling by Canada's
Supreme Court that Quebec had no right to secede unilaterally,
Parliament passed the "Clarity Act" in 2000, which required that any
vote in favor of independence be by a "clear majority," and that the
wording in any question be explicit in calling for Quebec's secession.

[This raises the question of what would happen if Quebec's voters
supported independence only by a narrow majority or if the wording of
the question was considered to be ambiguous.]{.underline} If Canada's
federal government rejected such a result, [would Quebec declare
independence unilaterally anyway? What would happen
afterward?]{.underline}

And if, in fact, Quebec convincingly voted for independence, complex
negotiations would be required on such economic issues as the division
of federal assets within Quebec and the share of Canada's national debt
that it would assume, whether Quebec would maintain a common central
bank and currency with what remained of Canada, and how their trade
relations would be managed.

Political issues to be resolved could include how an independent Quebec
would address the rights of Indigenous peoples that are currently
guaranteed by the federal government, as well as the rights of
non-Quebec citizens living in the new state and even Quebec's borders,
as it has never recognized that Labrador, to its northeast, is part of a
different province.

What Would the United States Do?

However, a PQ victory would also pose policy choices for the United
States. When, in 1995, it appeared that Quebec voters might support
independence, Bill Clinton made clear his support for, "a strong and
united Canada," doubtless seeing little benefit for U.S. interests from
the complications that would arise from the break-up of its northern
neighbor and the establishment of a new state on its borders.

In saying this, he was following in the footsteps of Jimmy Carter, who
won office in 1976, almost simultaneously with Quebec's first PQ
premier, René Lévesque. Carter made clear his "preference" for a united
Canada.

The unanswered questions for the United States could include whether an
independent Quebec would remain in NATO and, consequently, whether it
would create its armed forces to make at least some contribution to
Western defense at a time when American patience with perceived
free-riders on its military spending is wearing thin.

It would also need to determine if it would be part of the North
American Aerospace Defense Command (NORAD).

Quebec has a 505-mile-long border with four American states; the United
States would want strong assurances that its interests in countering
illegal immigration, narcotics smuggling, and terrorism would be
respected. And, in one way or another, U.S. trade policy would have to
account for the presence of this new state.

However, if the PQ wins and a referendum on Quebec's separation becomes
a real prospect, would Donald **[[Trump]{.underline}]{.mark}** feel the
same way as Presidents Carter and Clinton?

By inclination, he [[is a **disruptor**]{.underline}]{.mark} [and **may
see an opportunity** in the uncertainty that a change in North America's
political geography could offer. [He might **support
independence**]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} [[viewing the]{.mark}
small, newly created [state as **more likely to be responsive** to
American demands]{.mark}]{.underline}. [He may also believe [it could
offer him a]{.mark} greater [chance to **annex the rest of
Canada**]{.mark}]{.underline}, giving substance to his comments about it
becoming a fifty-first state.

Or, after stirring the pot sufficiently, he may decide, as did his
predecessors, that the status quo is preferable.

Whatever the outcome, [the return of the Quebec separatism issue would
surely add more twists and turns to the wild ride North America is
currently facing.]{.underline}

## ALL-PURPOSE

### Green Paradox\-\--2NC

#### [Green paradox]{.underline} turns emissions.

**de Graaf 23**, PhD, Associate Professor of International Politics at
the Ghent Institute for International and European Studies. (Thijs Van,
"10. Barrels, booms, and busts: the future of petrostates in a
decarbonizing world," in *Handbook on the Geopolitics of the Energy

2)

4.1 Race to Sell Oil

[[In response to]{.mark} the threat of **[peak]{.mark} oil
[demand]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[producer countries could]{.mark}
[**battle it out**,]{.mark} fight for market share,
[and]{.mark}]{.underline} try to [**[sell]{.mark} as much** of [their
oil]{.mark} now **[before demand declines]{.mark}** as a result of a
switch to **clean**]{.underline}er **[energy]{.underline}** sources.
[[This is a strategy]{.mark} that **low-cost producers** like
**[Saudi]{.mark}**]{.underline} Arabia [[might]{.underline}]{.mark} be
tempted to [[pursue]{.underline}]{.mark}. In fact, in March 2020, the
Saudi Ministry of Energy instructed Saudi Aramco to increase its
production capacity from 12 to 13 mb/d (Saudi Aramco, 2020). [Saudi
Aramco's]{.underline} April 2019 [bond prospectus]{.underline}, released
ahead of its initial public offering, [reads like a strategy to be
the]{.underline} "[last man standing]{.underline}" [in the oil
market]{.underline} (Krane, 2021). Similarly, the United Arab Emirates'
Abu Dhabi National Oil Company (ADNOC) had plans to increase its
production capacity to 5 mb/d, up from 3.1 mb/d in October 2018 (Kerr,
2018).

The idea here is that [[**climate policy** and the **anticipated
depreciation** of reserves]{.underline} [**undermines** incentives to
**leave oil in the ground**]{.underline}]{.mark}[, instead [provoking
a]{.mark} "**[panic and pump]{.mark}**" **[strategy]{.mark}** to
monetize as much of those reserves **before** the window
closes]{.underline}. It is also built on a belief that [oil will
**remain indispensable** for some time to come, and that NOCs may be at
an advantage compared to international oil co]{.underline}mpanies, which
face mounting pressure from shareholders, courts, and public opinion.
However, by [rushing to develop reserves and make the most out of what
could be the last oil boom, [**price wars** may
**erupt**]{.mark}]{.underline} between oil exporters, thus
[**[depressing prices]{.mark}** and revenues for all. [It could lead
to]{.mark} "**[green paradox]{.mark}**" **[effects]{.mark}**,
[where]{.mark}by [lower oil prices **slow**]{.mark}]{.underline} down
**[[the]{.mark} energy [transition]{.mark}]{.underline}** itself (Sinn,
2012). Moreover, [rapacious oil extraction is a short-term strategy
which does not improve long-term prospects for growth]{.underline} (van
der Ploeg, 2016).

### Green Paradox\-\--AT: Long-Term Contracts\-\--2NC

#### Plants can accelerate processing [even]{.underline} when locked into [long-term contracts]{.underline}.

**Grant 8-30**-2024, PhD, Professor of Sociology and Fellow of the
Renewable and Sustainable Energy Institute at the University of Colorado
Boulder. (Don, *et al*, "A worldwide analysis of stranded fossil fuel
assets' impact on power plants' CO2 emissions," Nature Communications,
Volume 15, p. 2, https://www.nature.com/articles/s41467-024-52036-8.pdf)

To date, [the study that comes closest to generating]{.underline} such
[evidence is [Di Maria]{.mark} et al]{.underline}.24 [empirical
examination of whether]{.underline} U.S. power [plants emitted more
sulfur dioxide]{.underline} (SO2) [between]{.underline} the
[announcement of]{.underline} the [Acid Rain]{.underline} Program
[and]{.underline} its [implementation]{.underline}. Consistent with the
green paradox thesis, [they [found]{.mark}]{.underline} that owners of
coal deposits responded to this program by extracting more coal and
selling it at a cheaper price. However, contrary to that thesis, price
decreases had no effect on plants' SO2 emissions, which the authors
attribute to the fact that [most [plants are **locked** into
**long-term**]{.mark}]{.underline} coal [**[purchases]{.mark}** that
prevent them from taking advantage of changes in spot
pricing.]{.underline}

Although their study casts doubt on the green paradox hypothesis, [**[Di
Maria]{.mark}** et al]{.underline}. also **[[overlook several
factors]{.underline}]{.mark}** [that]{.underline} beg further
investigation and [suggest]{.underline} how [climate policies might
still inadvertently increase emissions]{.underline}. Besides not
examining the CO2 emissions of power plants in the U.S. and throughout
the world, [their study does not measure the value of a nation's fossil
fuel assets that are at-risk of being **stranded**]{.underline}.
Instead, it examines whether the effect of coal prices on emissions
changes as the Acid Rain program came closer to being implemented.
Hence, it does not capture how at-risk assets are distributed unevenly
across the globe and threaten to disrupt the economies of some countries
more than others. In addition, [Di Maria et al. confined their study to
large, base-load plants that operate at full capacity almost all the
time]{.underline}. Consequently, it does not explore how [other
[plants]{.mark} may **[adjust their capacity]{.mark}
factor**]{.underline}25 [[when]{.mark} many of the **[reserves]{.mark}**
they have **already purchased** are [in peril]{.mark}.]{.underline}

In this work, [[we use a **worldwide dataset**]{.mark} on]{.underline}
individual [power plants' CO2 emission levels]{.underline} (annual
tonnes of CO2 emitted)26, their operational characteristics, and the
value of their countries' potentially stranded fossil fuel reserves27 to
fill these gaps [and conduct an **empirical analysis** of the effects of
at-risk fossil fuel assets on plants' carbon pollution under a 1.5
°C]{.underline} (and, in Supplementary Table 1, 2 °C) climate
stabilization scenario. [We test two]{.underline} simple, alternative
[models of the **green paradox**]{.underline}. [The first predicts that
plants will pollute at higher levels in countries with more at-risk
fossil fuel reserves]{.underline} because those countries are more
likely to exercise regulatory leniency to soften the otherwise
disruptive effects of stranded assets on government revenues,
employment, and energy security. Such [leniency allows plants to **defer
maintenance**]{.underline}, **[exploit loopholes]{.underline}** in
environmental regulation, [and delay adopting **cleaner
tech**]{.underline}nologies, all of which directly add to a plant's
carbon pollution. [The second model assumes that most plants are
**locked** into **long-term fossil**]{.underline} fuel
**[contracts]{.underline}** and many of the fuels they have acquired are
derived from their host country's carbon reserves. [It
predicts]{.underline} that [in countries with more at-risk assets,
[plants]{.mark}]{.underline} have a vested interest in
**[[shift]{.underline}]{.mark}**ing the [[**processing** of **fossil
fuels forward** to capitalize on]{.mark} their purchases of **coal**,
**[oil]{.mark}**, [and **gas**]{.mark} and use their plant equipment
while they]{.underline} still [**can**. [By incentivizing plants to
**speed up**]{.mark} **their [operations]{.mark}**]{.underline} (i.e.,
[increase their capacity utilization rate]{.underline}) in this way,
[[stranded assets]{.underline}]{.mark} indirectly [[cause]{.mark}
plants' [emissions to **rise**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Joskow28 provides
several examples of how [[plants **subject to long-term contracts**
can]{.mark} **effectively [accelerate]{.mark}** the **[burning]{.mark}**
of already purchased fuels [by **renegotiating quantity
provisions**]{.mark}]{.underline} at dates specified within these
agreements. Consistent with our two predictions, [findings]{.underline}
here [indicate]{.underline} that [not only do power plants release more
CO2 [in countries where]{.mark} more fossil fuel [assets are in
jeopardy]{.mark} of being stranded, but]{.underline} in those same
countries, [[plants]{.mark} also [operate **closer to**]{.mark} **full
[capacity]{.mark}**]{.underline}, causing them to emit CO2 at even
higher levels. Results thus point to countries' regulatory leniency and
plants' vested interest in their long-term contracts as the mechanisms
that might lead to a green paradox. Unlike most past studies that rely
on simulation methods, our study also uses a hierarchical linear model
(HLM) that rejects specifications that cannot reproduce observations and
estimates the magnitude of parameters29. This technique allows us not
just to empirically assess whether at-risk assets are associated with
plants' emissions, but also to calculate the total volume of CO2 that
plants emit in response to those assets. Results suggest that in the
case of countries like the United States and Russia, the extra emissions
attributed to the stranded asset effect exhaust a significant percent of
their electricity sectors' remaining carbon budgets.

### UQ\-\--AT: Peak Oil Demand\-\--2NC

#### Renewable growth [only]{.underline} supplants fossil fuels if it's accompanied by a carbon tax. 

**Porter 14**, Economics reporter for the business section of The New
York Times. (Eduardo, 11-18-2014, \"A Carbon Tax Could Bolster Green
Energy\". *NY Times*,
https://www.nytimes.com/2014/11/19/business/economy/a-carbon-tax-could-bolster-wobbly-progress-in-renewable-energy.html)

But [for all the optimism generated by **[cheaper
renewable]{.mark}**]{.underline} fuel[**[s]{.mark}**, they **[do
not]{.mark}**, [**on their own**, put the world on the **low-carbon
path**]{.mark}]{.underline} necessary to keep climate change in check.

[Progress is **faltering**]{.underline} on several fronts. [The
precipitous fall in the prices of photovoltaic cells]{.underline} from
2008 to 2012 pretty much [stopped]{.underline} in 2013, [after **rapid
consolidation**]{.underline} of the industry.

The International Energy Agency now projects that installed global
nuclear capacity in 2025 will fall 5 percent, to 24 percent below what
will be needed to stay on the safe side of climate change. And **[carbon
capture technologies]{.underline}**, which will be essential if the
world is to keep consuming any form of fossil fuel, [remain **hampered
by high costs, meager investment** and **scant political
commitment**]{.underline}.

"The unrelenting rise in coal use without deployment of carbon capture
and storage is fundamentally incompatible with climate change
objectives," noted the International Energy Agency in its Technology
Perspectives report.

Despite the falling costs of renewable energy in the United States, the
Energy Information Administration's baseline assumptions project that in
2040 only 16.5 percent of electricity generation will come from
renewable energy sources, up from some 13 percent today. More than
two-thirds will come from coal and gas. Without some carbon capture and
storage technology, drastic climate change is almost certainly
unavoidable.

What is necessary to get us on a safer path?

White House officials trust that the administration has the tools,
including fuel economy and appliance efficiency standards, [the
**[E]{.mark}**]{.underline}nvironmental
**[[P]{.underline}]{.mark}**rotection
**[[A]{.underline}]{.mark}**gency's new limits on power plant emissions
and regulations to limit other greenhouse gases.

Yet the Energy Information Administration's
**[[projections]{.underline}]{.mark}** suggest how hard the task will
be. Though they were developed before the Environmental Protection
Agency issued its new rules, they [[included hypothetical
outlines]{.underline}]{.mark} that could mimic some of its effects. In
one, coal power plants were decommissioned more quickly; in another,
subsidies to renewable energy were kept until 2040. In another, [[the
price of renewables **fell fast**]{.underline}]{.mark}er than expected.
**[[None]{.underline}]{.mark}** of them [[did much to **move the carbon
dial**.]{.underline}]{.mark}

[[There is **one tool available**]{.mark} to trim carbon emissions on a
**relevant scale:** [a **carbon tax**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline} That
solution, however, remains off the table.

[[If]{.mark} a carbon tax were to be imposed]{.underline} next year,
starting at \$25 and rising by 5 percent a year, the Energy Information
Administration estimates, [carbon dioxide emissions from American power
plants would fall to]{.underline} only 419 million tons by 2040, about
**[one-fifth of where they are today.]{.underline}** Total carbon
dioxide emissions from energy in the United States would fall to 3.6
billion tons --- 1.8 billion tons less than today. [By providing a
monetary incentive, economists say, such [a tax would]{.mark} offer by
far the most effective way to **[encourage
business]{.mark}**]{.underline} and individuals [[to **reduce** their
use of fossil fuels]{.mark} and invest in alternatives]{.underline}.

#### Clean energy will grow, but [so will demand]{.underline}. Fossil fuels will [stick]{.underline}. 

**Wald 8-2**-2024, Energy Analyst, Breakthrough Institute; former policy
analyst and communications consultant at the Nuclear Energy Institute.
(Matthew L., \"Clean Energy May be Growing, But It's Not Replacing Dirty
Energy,\" Breakthrough Journal,
https://thebreakthroughjournal.substack.com/p/clean-energy-may-be-growing-but-its)

[Lots of people have high hopes for an energy transition, but they're
looking through the wrong end of the telescope]{.underline}.

[Yes, solar farms are spreading out]{.underline} over deserts and farm
fields, [and wind turbines are popping up]{.underline} all over the land
and even in a few locations offshore. But global consumption of fossil
fuels grew 1.5 percent last year, according to the Statistical Review of
World Energy. [[Clean energy is growing, but **so is demand**]{.mark},
and to meet it,]{.underline} [[**dirty energy** is growing too]{.mark}.
[**We're going to have to do things differently** if we hope to
**trim**]{.mark} the **[output]{.mark}** of climate-changing
emissions]{.underline}.

Even the fossil mix is getting worse; [[oil was up]{.mark} even
[**faster** than total fossil consumption, and demand
passed]{.mark}]{.underline} **[[100 million barrels a
day]{.underline}]{.mark}** for the first time. Coal also hit a new
record, and carbon emissions from energy consumption are up by 50
percent since the turn of the century. The fossil share of primary
energy slipped by 0.4 percent, to 81.5 percent, but the climate doesn't
care about percentages; it cares about volumes.

The Statistical Review of World Energy might be more familiar by its
older name, the BP Statistical Review. The recent history of BP
illustrates the waxing and waning of the idea of green energy. It used
to be "British Petroleum" but rebranded itself as "Beyond Petroleum" in
2001, and then reverted back to "BP" in 2006. It published the report, a
survey of all forms of energy production and consumption, for 70 years,
and the Statistical Review has always served as a useful guide to all
things oil.

But BP dropped the report in 2023. A spokesman for the company told
Reuters that BP wanted to give the report team more time to concentrate
on a shift to renewables and low-carbon energy. Now the report is funded
by BP, KPMG, and Kearney. It's still using the same methodology, and
some of the analysts who produce it have left BP to continue on the data
series.

The race to transition to zero emissions by mid-century was going to
have to be a sprint. Now it appears that [the starting gun has fired and
the runners are milling around in confusion.]{.underline}

"I would argue that [[the transition **hasn't even crossed the start
line**]{.underline}]{.mark}," said one of those analysts, Nick Wayth,
now the chief executive of the Energy Institute. He spoke at a briefing
on the report at the Center for Strategic and International Studies in
Washington, DC.

The perception here in the United States is that renewable energy is
booming. The United States has ample capital, a strong industrial base
and a grid to plug into, plus government incentives. But in 2022, solar
and wind together produced about 13.66 percent of the kilowatt-hours in
the U.S.; in 2023, the last year for which full statistics are
available, they produced about 14.1 percent of U.S. electricity
generation. These numbers are strong but will leave us a long way from
zero at mid-century. And while U.S. greenhouse gas emissions may have
declined by 1.9 percent last year, global emissions increased by about
1.1 percent.

Why are emissions increasing? Because [[the **world is hungry**
for]{.underline}]{.mark} two things: [[**energy** and]{.mark} all the
**[manufactured goods]{.mark}** that consume energy]{.underline} (cars
and trucks, refrigerators, water pumps, cell phones, etc.).

#### Peak oil demand is [half a century]{.underline} away. 

**Messler 24**, oilfield veteran. Citing Arjun Murti, a former Goldman
Sachs energy analyst. (David, 5-6-2024, \"Why the IEA is Wrong About
Peak Oil Demand,\" OilPrice,
https://oilprice.com/Energy/Crude-Oil/Why-the-IEA-is-Wrong-About-Peak-Oil-Demand.html)
**\*BOPD = barrels of oil per day**

[It is fairly common]{.underline} nowadays [to see]{.underline}
relatively near-term [estimates for]{.underline} a point at which demand
for petroleum-based fuels begins to decline. The term often used to
describe this "tipping point" is **[Peak Oil Demand]{.underline}**. When
I say "near term," I mean right around the corner if you look at an
estimate published last year by the International Energy Agency-IEA, an
intergovernmental agency headquartered in Paris, France, and originally
established after the Oil Embargo of 1973 to help cushion against future
oil shocks. This agency has expanded its mission to a fairly broad remit
over the years since, and it is not the purpose of this article to
detail all its endeavors. One role we will highlight is that of the one
it plays in gauging and advising member governments on energy security
and energy sources for the coming years.

In that capacity, the IEA in a report entitled, Oil 2023, and published
last year settled on 2028 as the year past which the use of petroleum
fuels will begin to decline.

"Growth in the world's demand for oil is set to slow almost to a halt in
the coming years, with the high prices and security of supply concerns
highlighted by the global energy crisis hastening the shift towards
cleaner energy technologies, according to a new IEA report released
today."

This view is largely shared, particularly with respect to liquid motor
fuels, by other agencies and organizations that produce long range
estimates. The U.S. Energy Information Agency-EIA, Rystad, and Det
Norske Veritas- DNV, all show this category tailing off rapidly in the
2030s as electric vehicles assume larger shares of passenger vehicles.
We will call this the "Bear Case" for liquid fuels.

As you might expect the Organization of Petroleum Exporting
Countries-OPEC, disagrees with this view. In fact in their recent report
on oil demand outlook, published in Nov 2023, they see oil demand of all
kinds, except for electricity generation, rising from \~105 mm BOPD in
2025, to 116 mm BOPD in 2045. This forecast show use of oil as a road
fuel continuing to be the largest source of demand increase for this
period.

The report notes that "the divergence between the IEA and OPEC outlooks
is largely due to assumptions regarding the speed at which internal
combustion engine vehicles will be replaced by electric vehicles."

What is interesting is that [[it is]{.mark} very difficult, if not
**[impossible]{.mark}**, [to see a]{.mark} production **[trend]{.mark}**
being established [that would **support** the **bear
case**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [In the U.S., [we are pumping]{.mark} at a
rate of over **[13]{.mark}.2 mm [BOPD]{.mark}** and still importing
\~6.7 mm BOPD]{.underline} to feed our nearly 22 mm BOPD daily habit.
The U.S. Energy Information Agency-EIA forecasts in their monthly
Short-Term Energy Outlook-STEO that by the end of 2025, global
production and demand fall into a fairly tight balance at 105 mm BOPD.
That certainly isn't a long-term trend, but as is often said, the
long-term trend is made up of a bunch of short-term ones. For my part, I
would say that [the trend line in the STEO graph]{.underline} below
[matches the **OPEC estimate**]{.underline} more closely than the other
three.

Both of these notions cannot be true. Which is the correct assumption
about future oil demand? Or are they both wrong? What are two factors
these two disparate views of oil demand are not taking into account?

[The **[first]{.mark}** answer lies in how you interpret the [**growth**
of the **middle class** in **China**, **India**, and
Africa]{.mark}]{.underline} in terms of energy demand and the final form
it will take. [The [**second** is]{.mark} the advent of]{.underline}
energy demand for Artificial Intelligence
(**[[AI]{.underline}]{.mark}**), [[an **entirely new** source of
**demand**]{.mark} that is just now starting to appear in energy
**demand forecasts**]{.underline}. I discussed one possible outcome of
this demand for U.S. natural gas in an article in March 2024.

To be clear, I am not arguing that AI demand will directly impact crude
oil demand as a primary source. Most analysts are factoring renewables
and natural gas to meet AI demand. What will impact demand for WTI and
other baskets of crude is the relationship to light oil production in
the U.S. and the associated gas that's produced along with it. We will
leave that discussion for a future article and refocus on our basic
topic. What could oil demand actually be when accounting for growth in
currently underserved but upwardly aspiring lower classes?

[Then there is the **Bull Case** for **oil**]{.underline}. Arjun Murti,
a well-known energy commentator and partner at energy analyst firm

250 mm BOPD, using a reference point of 10 bbls a year!

Where are we now? The U.S consumes \~22 bbls of oil annually per capita
while China consumes 3.7 bbls per capita. Indians use just 1.3 bbls per
annum. That's a pretty wide gap, and as Arjun notes, "economic [growth

economic growth without adequate energy."

[One of the arguments put forward by the **Peak Oil** crowd is that

**[[Oil]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

Looking at Arjun's graph below, which uses China as an example, we can

A point that leads me to what Arjun noted as the ultimate demand limiter
and why, although countries that will surely desire to increase their

Quoting Arjun, "There is no precedent for countries importing 20-30 mm
BOPD" to meet their energy needs. The U.S., before the advent of shale
production was importing over 10 mm BOPD as recently as 2005. That's
what we know is possible.

35-45 mm BOPD. We just don't know if this can be done from both a

highlights global oil production has increased only about 3 mm BOPD
since 2019. In order [for the world's poor to become richer, a great

Your takeaway

in the constant blare of climate change and energy transition noise.

#### Oil demand is more likely to [plateau]{.underline} than [peak]{.underline}.

**Blas 23**, MA, Bloomberg Opinion columnist covering energy and
commodities. (Javier, 9-25-2023, \"With Fossil Fuels, 'Peak Demand'
Isn't What It Sounds Like,\" Bloomberg,
https://www.bloomberg.com/opinion/articles/2023-09-25/with-fossil-fuels-peak-demand-isn-t-what-it-sounds-like)

Last week, [IEA Executive Director]{.underline} Fatih [Birol
announced]{.underline} that the next edition of the report, expected in
October, would show the world is on what he called "the cusp of a
historic turning point": [[Demand for]{.underline}]{.mark} coal,
**[[oil]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and natural [gas [is set to
**peak**]{.mark}]{.underline} before 2030.

![A graph with a line going up Description automatically
generated](media/image2.png){width="5.208784995625547in"
height="2.875249343832021in"}

Over the years, I have learned the hard way that when it comes to the
energy market, there's much truth in the cliché "it is difficult to make
predictions, especially about the future." If the prediction is about an
inflection point, then it's even more difficult.

The IEA should have learned the lesson, too. Historically, it
underestimated --- and by quite a lot --- the growth of wind and solar;
it all but missed the birth of the US oil shale revolution; and
prematurely called the end of coal's golden age in China by at least a
decade (and still counting).

Peak fossil-fuel consumption will be a cause for celebration. But
[**[I'm skeptical]{.mark}** it's]{.underline} as [near]{.underline} as
the IEA has said, especially for gas. Interestingly, Birol hedged his
words quite carefully: "Although fossil fuels are set to hit their peaks
this decade in structural terms, there can still be spikes, dips and
plateaus on the way down."

Here, "structural terms" does quite a lot of heavy lifting. And if there
can be spikes in demand after the peak, well, let's not call the first
high point a peak. Because it wouldn't be.

The debate about when the peak would be reached is intriguing --- but,
to me, it's a distraction. We wouldn't know for sure until several years
after demand has actually peaked. [[What really matters is the **shape
of the curve**]{.mark} in the years before and [after demand reaches its
**zenith**]{.mark}]{.underline}. The amateur mountaineer in me sees a
peak as the beginning of a frequently quite abrupt descent. One treks
higher, suffering and sweating, pauses a bit at the mount's summit and
then enjoys an easier downward climb. With most mountain ranges, going
up is as steep as coming down. That's what "peak" sounds like to many
others.

Not in the world of energy.

For oil, coal and gas, [[the fall may be **gentle** and
**slow**]{.underline}]{.mark}. After summiting, for a hiker, it's like
the other side of mountain turns into a high-altitude plateau rather
than your typically rapid Alpine descent. For many years, [[fossil-fuel
consumption may **hover** close to its **maximum
point**]{.underline}]{.mark}. How long? Maybe five or 10 years or even
as long as **[two decades]{.underline}**. That's incompatible with
what's needed for the world to achieve its net-zero emissions target by
2050. Equally, [[demand growth may **slow**]{.underline}]{.mark} to a
crawl for several years [[before reaching its]{.mark} ultimate
[peak]{.mark}]{.underline}, wrong-footing those bullish fossil-fuel
investors who bet on continued historical growth rates.

The history of fossil-fuel consumption suggest the years after the peak
wouldn't be the expectation of a quick descent.

[Take **coal**]{.underline}. [The IEA initially thought that
demand]{.underline} for the world's dirtiest fuel [had reached an apex
in **2013**]{.underline}. Since then, consumption has largely leveled
off and last year started climbing again, setting a fresh peak. [Under
current trends, demand is likely to set a **fresh all-time
high**]{.underline} in 2024 and 2025.

The gap between the most likely consumption path after reaching the
crest and the net-zero models is incredibly wide --- so broad that
discussing the exact date of the peak is redundant.

[Consider **oil**]{.underline}. [The IEA itself says]{.underline} global
[[oil demand needs to **drop**]{.mark} to 75 million
barrels]{.underline} a day by 2030 to be on track to achieve net zero.
[[It's **impossible** to see that happening]{.underline}]{.mark}. Why?
Because [demand is more than **103 million** barrels now]{.underline},
[and [**all** suggests **continued growth**]{.mark} over the next five
years]{.underline}, reaching 105 million to 106 million barrels a day by
2028 or 2029. Even if that marks the oil market's peak, the gulf between
reality and the model would exceed 30 million barrels a day --- equal to
the combined daily consumption of China, India, Japan, Germany, the UK,
France and Italy.

The day when the world's use of fossil fuels reaches its maximum is
nearer than ever. For oil and coal, perhaps it's as close as the IEA
says. For gas, it's further away. But the eye-catching bit is on the
summit's other side. And, I'm afraid, that [[anyone]{.mark} [thinking
about]{.mark} a **[rapid decline]{.mark}** after an extended ascension
[would be **disappointed**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

### L\-\--Stranded Assets\-\--2NC

#### [Stranded assets]{.underline}\-\--the plan's a [policy shock]{.underline} that [devalues]{.underline} oil. 

**Sen 20**, \*PhD, Assistant Professor at the Environmental Economics
and Natural Resources Group at Wageningen University. \*\*PhD, Deputy
Director of the ifo Center for Energy, Climate, and Resources. (\*Suphi
and \*\*Marie-Theres von Schickfus, March 2020, \"Climate policy,
stranded assets, and investors' expectations,\" Journal of Environmental
Economics and Management, Volume 100,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0095069618307083#!)

As early as 2012, global financial services companies drew attention to
the risk of coal investments becoming stranded as a consequence of the
2°C "carbon budget."1 This carbon budget specifies the maximal amount of
cumulative carbon emissions that can be emitted without surpassing a 2°C
temperature increase above preindustrial levels (Meinshausen et al.,
2009; Allen et al., 2009). Therefore, [[climate policies might render
fossil-fuel assets **worthless**]{.mark} prior to the end of their
economic life time]{.underline}. We study whether the current market
valuation of companies owning fossil fuel assets reflects this risk of
stranding assets.2 A failure to price in this risk can lead to costly
consequences for the whole economy. First, the resulting misallocation
of capital due to delayed divestment could render the transition to
clean capital more expensive (IPCC, 2014; IRENA, 2017a). Second, [[a
**sudden** and **unexpected tightening** of]{.mark} carbon **[emission
policies]{.mark}**]{.underline} (Batten et al., 2016) or sudden changes
in expectations in the presence of tipping points (Krugman, 1991) [[can
lead to **abrupt repricing**]{.mark} of fossil fuel assets.
[This]{.mark}]{.underline} situation [[can result in a **negative supply
shock** through changes in **energy use** and **second-round**
effects]{.mark} in financial markets]{.underline}.3 Financial
institutions such as the Bank of England, the Dutch Central Bank (DNB),
the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB), and the European Systemic
Risk Board (ESRB) have identified the mispricing of stranded asset risk
as a potential systemic risk and threat to financial stability.4

Therefore, we analyze the interaction between investors' expectations
and the development of climate policies. Investors' reactions to new
policies depend on their prior expectations, which, in turn, are shaped
by previous policies. This interaction is central to the current paper:
What are investors' priors regarding stranded asset risk, and (how) do
these priors change when climate policy proposals are announced? In
particular, we analyze (i) whether investors have already priced in
expected losses due to the carbon budget, (ii) whether they only respond
to concrete policies, and (iii) whether they expect firms to be
financially compensated for stranded assets. To answer these questions,
we exploit the gradual development of a climate policy proposal in
Germany targeting lignite assets and investigate how adjustments of this
proposal have affected the market valuation of firms active in
electricity production. We find that investors did not react to
announcements of the initial "climate levy" proposal, which was directed
at stranding lignite assets by charging an extra fee on carbon emissions
(Stage 1). Investors also did not respond when the proposal transformed
into a compensation mechanism (Stage 2), paying plant owners for not
running their units. Only announcements that the compensation mechanism
may not go through due to violating state aid rules (Stage 3) resulted
in a significant and negative reaction. Our findings show that investors
do care about the stranded asset risk, but with an expectation of a
compensation mechanism.

Our analysis starts from the notion that the evolution of climate
policies and the expectations of investors are interrelated. First,
climate policies and policy proposals provide signals that shape how the
investors perceive the stranded asset risk. [[For instance]{.mark},
**setting [a price]{.mark}** [on **CO2 emissions**]{.mark}]{.underline}
or imposing a cost on fossil resource extraction5 can [[reduce
demand]{.mark}, **[slow]{.mark}** down **[investment]{.mark}** in fossil
infrastructure, [and cause **asset stranding**]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Alternatively, policies addressing fossil-fuel reductions may compensate
fossil-fuel owners for leaving their reserves unburned. For example,
Harstad (2012) proposed that, in the absence of a global climate
agreement, "the coalition's best policy is to simply buy foreign
deposits and conserve them".6 Second, investors\' reactions to policy
signals depend on their prior expectations regarding the likelihood of
asset stranding and the credibility of climate policy announcements. For
example, they may have already devalued assets following information on
the carbon budget implied by the Paris Agreement, or they may find it
difficult to translate the concept of a carbon budget into stranded
asset risk.7 In the latter case, they would wait for further information
on climate policies with clear asset stranding implications. Even the
announcement of climate policies does not necessarily lead investors to
reassess the likeliness of asset stranding, if they expect a
compensation mechanism. The policy proposal we investigate provides the
opportunity to disentangle the effects of these policy signals and
expectations. By tracking the stock market response to different stages
of the proposal, we can draw conclusions about investors\' prior
expectations and how they evolved in the course of the policy\'s
development.3

### !\-\--Conventional War\-\--2NC

#### War causes extinction even it [never escalates]{.underline}.

**Taylor 23**, PhD, Guest Lecturer in Peace and Conflict Studies at the
University of Innsbruck, Austria. (Noah B., "Great Powers Conflict," in
*Existential Risks in Peace and Conflict Studies*, p. 68-69, Palgrave
Macmillan, ISSN 2752-857X)

The literature on Great Power Conflicts frames it as an indirect or a
compounding risk. Unlike a cataclysmic asteroid, it is difficult to
imagine how such a conflict would directly lead to human extinction. The
indirect pathways are easier to imagine. A Great Powers Conflict could
result in an intentional or unintended nuclear strike, likely resulting
in retaliation and nuclear war. In an even less direct manner, a [[Great
Power Conflict would]{.underline}]{.mark} likely [[result in a
**breakdown of trust**]{.mark} at the global level, [making
**coop**]{.mark}]{.underline}eration [[on]{.mark} **other**]{.underline}
possible **[[existential risks]{.underline}]{.mark}** [[like
climate]{.mark} change **[difficult]{.mark}**]{.underline}.
[[War]{.mark},]{.underline} especially at this scale, significantly
[[directs **resources** and **attention**]{.underline}]{.mark} toward it
and [[away from other issues]{.mark}. This decrease in the overall
**global resilience**]{.underline} to deal with threats to existence
[could]{.underline} feasibly [happen at levels of extreme geopolitical
tension]{.underline} without necessarily requiring the outbreak of a
violent conflict. Thus, it is imaginable that the overall amount of
conflict, directly violent or not, between Great Powers increases the
potential for other threats to rise to the level of threatening our
existence.

How Bad Could It Be?

In the Founders Pledge report on Great Powers Conflicts, Clare (2021)
describes three possible existential risk scenarios that could result
from such conflicts. The first is a Great Power Conflict increasing
other potential existential risks. A foreseeable pathway to this
scenario is where **[heightened tensions]{.underline}** or outright
conflict between Great Powers [breakdown **international**]{.underline}
cooperation and **[coordination]{.underline}** that would have otherwise

[would be]{.underline} **[tensions]{.underline}** or conflicts between
Great Powers Conflict **[inhibiting coop]{.underline}**eration [from
addressing]{.underline} extreme **[climate change]{.underline}**, [which
becomes]{.underline} an **[existential]{.underline}** threat (the
likelihood of climate change resulting in an existential risk will be
addressed in Chap. 5). Similarly, [the state of
[insecurity]{.mark}]{.underline} created by this breakdown of trust
[[could **direct**]{.underline}]{.mark} more [[**investment** into
tech]{.underline}]{.mark}nological [[research]{.underline}]{.mark} that

### !\-\--AT: Emboldenment\-\--2NC

#### Emboldenment is [backwards]{.underline}. Low prices cause resource grabs, shows of strength, and diversionary war\-\--high prices strengthen deterrence. 

**Blankenship 24**, PhD, Assistant Professor of Political Science at the
University of Miami. (Brian, Qaraman Hasan, Soran Mohtadi, Indra
Overland, and Johannes Urpelainen, 5-17-2024, "Oil Prices and
International Conflict: Why Low Oil Revenue May Not Pacify Petrostates,"
International Interactions, 50:3, p. 482-485, DOI:
10.1080/03050629.2024.2352486)

But while the prevalence of "oil wars" is contested, [an emerging
consensus suggests]{.underline} that regardless of whether countries
fight over oil, [[petroleum revenues]{.underline}]{.mark} nevertheless
**[[embolden petrostates]{.underline}]{.mark}** to fight more broadly.
The basic logic of the "Emboldenment" argument is that oil revenue
allows petrostates to behave more aggressively by giving them more
freedom to invest in waging war (**[[Ashford]{.underline}]{.mark}**
2022). **[[Colgan]{.underline}]{.mark}** (2010), for example, argues
that revolutionary leaders with revisionist ambitions are more likely to
initiate conflict when they have access to plentiful oil revenues, such
as Saddam Hussein's Iraq or Ayatollah Khomeini's Iran.

Notably, the Emboldenment mechanism as most notably developed by Colgan
was intended to explain differences in the conflict initiation behavior
of petrostates and non-petrostates---not to explore how variation in oil
revenue over time shapes variation in petrostate behavior. Nevertheless,
if one extends the logic of the Emboldenment mechanism to oil prices,
one might expect petrostates to be more likely to initiate conflict when
oil prices are higher. For countries that depend on oil as a source of
government revenue, changes in demand for and the price of oil have
dramatic implications for their ability to provide public goods,
distribute patronage, and invest in military and economic instruments of
foreign policy. Thus, more plentiful revenue can allow petrostates to
indulge their foreign policy ambitions. By contrast, revenue shortfalls
should constrain petrostate foreign policy, generating incentives for
petrostate to bide their time and live to fight another day by
conserving resources (MacDonald and Parent 2018). The Emboldenment
school, then, might expect higher oil prices to be associated with more
petro-aggression.

Why Low Oil Prices May Not Pacify Petrostates

Yet even if their oil wealth enables petrostates with revisionist
ambitions to initiate more conflict, on average, than non-petrostates,
[it does **not**]{.underline} necessarily **[follow]{.underline}**
[that]{.underline} the effect of temporal fluctuations in that oil
wealth will be in the same direction---that is, that **[higher oil
prices]{.underline}** will necessarily [mean **more** petrostate
**conflict** initiation]{.underline}. [**Constrained resources** due to
declines to]{.underline} their [oil wealth]{.underline} may [lead
petrostates to **go on the offensive** in a desperate effort to **stave
off decline**]{.underline}, [discourage other countries from **preying
on**]{.underline} their **[weakness]{.underline}**, [and **distract**
domestic audiences]{.underline}. This is in effect the inverse of the
Emboldenment mechanism, which expects that oil revenue empowers
petrostates to initiate conflict because they can. Instead, drops in
their oil revenue may compel petrostates to initiate conflict because
they feel they must.

Skeptics of the Emboldenment school argue that [petrostates have **good
reason** to be **cautious** about attacking their
neighbors]{.underline}. Even if they win, [war and]{.underline} any
subsequent [occupation]{.underline} of conquered [territory is
**costly**]{.underline}, and may be especially so for petrostates.
[Vital oil [infrastructure may be damaged due to
fighting]{.mark}]{.underline} in contested territory or direct attacks
from adversary forces, and any foreign investment that could help
rebuild this infrastructure may dry up. Moreover, because
[[petrostates]{.underline}]{.mark} typically depend on their oil
exports, they [[are]{.mark} highly [vulnerable to]{.mark} international
**[sanctions]{.mark}**]{.underline} imposed as a result of their
aggression (Meierding 2020; Jang and Smith 2021). Indeed, if the war
goes badly, petrostates risk having their own oil resources conquered by
their adversary, which may be even more inclined to seize them when
prices are high (Caselli, Morelli, and Rohner 2015).

[[When oil prices **decline**]{.mark}, **however**,
[petrostates]{.mark}]{.underline} may feel compelled to [[**throw
caution** to the **wind**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Low oil prices could
increase conflict through at least three different mechanisms: battling
over a smaller market, diversionary conflict, and signaling strength.
First, as Pischedda (2020) suggests, [actors facing a]{.underline}n
acute [**window of vulnerability** may]{.underline} be tempted to
**[seize]{.underline}** the **[resources]{.underline}** of another
[actor to stave off decline.]{.underline}5 This is the Battling over a
Smaller Market mechanism, and is similar to the dynamics of what
Meierding (2020) calls "oil gambits": oil conflicts fought out of
desperation. Markowitz and others argue that this sort of conquest is
particularly likely among countries that depend on rents extracted from
land, and when the resources in question are more easily lootable
(Markowitz 2020; Markowitz et al. 2020; Coe and Markowitz 2021). Altman
(2017, 2020) similarly shows that conquest attempts since 1945 tend to
be characterized by sudden land seizures (faits accomplis) rather than
through threats.

The Battling over a Smaller Market mechanism would expect greater
petrostate conflict initiation when oil prices are lower, and would in
particular predict aggression toward other petrostates and oil-rich
territory. The logic here is that [[when faced with a **negative
shock**]{.mark} to their oil revenues, [petrostates]{.mark}
may]{.underline} try to [**reverse** their fortunes. Petrostates
[might]{.mark}]{.underline} expect that
**[[seiz]{.underline}]{.mark}**ing additional [**oil
[resources]{.mark}** will help them weather]{.underline} the impact of
[low]{.underline} oil [prices]{.underline} for two reasons. The first is
[[by]{.underline}]{.mark} simply [[**increasing**
the]{.underline}]{.mark} total [[amount of]{.underline}]{.mark} oil
[[revenue they bring in]{.underline}]{.mark} each year (Meierding 2022).
The second is by giving the petrostate a greater share of global
production, and thus allowing them more influence on the oil market and
greater ability to shape prices unilaterally (Bordoff 2020). During the
low oil price era of the 1990s and early 2000s, for example, Iran, Iraq,
Nigeria, Trinidad and Tobago, and Venezuela all initiated disputes over
oil-rich territory.6

Second, [[petrostates]{.mark} might]{.underline} also elect to
**[[initiate]{.underline}]{.mark}** more [[**conflict** when]{.mark} oil
[prices are low to **distract domestic audiences**]{.mark}]{.underline}.
This is the Diversionary Conflict mechanism. Because reduced oil revenue
constrains petrostates' ability to distribute public goods and patronage
at home, [low oil prices can lead to anti-government
**opposition**]{.underline} and unrest. [To **sideline**]{.underline}
and ultimately reduce [public **dissatisfaction**, the government
might]{.underline} try to [generate a]{.underline}
"**[rally-around-the-flag]{.underline}**" [effect by
**[diverting]{.mark}** popular [ire toward a **foreign enemy** and
**stoking nationalism**]{.mark}]{.underline} (Lee 1977; Oakes 2012).
Like the Battling over a Smaller Market mechanism, the Diversionary
Conflict mechanism is based on a "gambling for resurrection" logic in
which petrostates might be willing to run great risks in the hopes of
staving off a negative outcome (Downs and Rocke 1994). But what matters
here is not so much the military or policy objectives of the war, like
in the Battling over a Smaller Market mechanism, but rather the
existence of the conflict itself, and the effects that a sense of common
struggle and victory over a common enemy has on policymakers' domestic
political fortunes (Meierding 2022). Some observers, for example,
speculate that [[**Russia's** decision to escalate]{.underline}]{.mark}
its involvement [[in **Syria** was]{.underline}]{.mark} at least partly
[[to **divert attention**]{.mark} from the country's economic woes
amidst **low oil prices**]{.underline} during the mid- and late-2010s
(Åslund 2016; Petkova 2020).

Finally, [**negative shocks** to]{.underline} their [oil revenue may
lead petrostates to **initiate**]{.underline} more [**conflict** to
**show strength**]{.underline}, via the Signaling Strength mechanism. [A
**large**]{.underline} body of **[lit]{.underline}**erature [points to
the emphasis]{.underline} that [states place on]{.underline} cultivating
**[reputations for strength]{.underline}** and resolve, as doing so can
allow them to more effectively use threats of force to deter and coerce
other countries (Schelling 1966). Scholars debate the degree to which
reputation "matters" in terms of whether adversaries believe that a
country's past behavior is a reliable predictor of its future behavior

However, even reputation skeptics concede that [states often believe
their reputation matters]{.underline}, [and act to build]{.underline}
and preserve [their reputation for **toughness**]{.underline} (Tang
2005; Yarhi-Milo 2018). Clare and Danilovic (2010) find that states are
more likely to initiate conflict when other states have reason to doubt
its willingness to fight.

By a similar logic, one might expect declines in oil wealth to lead

[**[weakness]{.mark}** and signal their **willingness to

aggression. Rather, they may pick targeted fights without necessarily

[[When]{.mark} oil [prices are high]{.mark}, **by contrast**,
[petrostates may be]{.mark} sufficiently [**self-confident** to

Quantitative Results

The presentation of the results proceeds as follows. First, we present
the main results showing the overall effect of oil prices on petrostate
conflict initiation. Second, we subject the results to several
additional checks to probe the robustness of the findings. Finally, we
probe the causal mechanisms by exploring the conditions under which the
link between oil prices and petrostate conflict initiation is stronger
or weaker.

Table 1 presents the main results using incidence rate ratios, where
values less than 1 indicate a negative effect and those above 1 a
positive effect. Across different lag specifications, [[oil prices have
a]{.underline}]{.mark} statistically
**[[significant]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [[**negative association**
with]{.mark} petrostate [conflict initiation]{.mark} for
MIDs]{.underline} at hostility levels of both 2 or higher and 4 or
higher. For example, Model 1's coefficient (0.987) indicates that
[[the]{.mark} predicted [number]{.mark} of MIDs [declines]{.mark} by
[**1.3%** for each **dollar increase**]{.mark} in]{.underline} the [oil
price]{.underline}. For ease of interpretation, Figure 1 presents the
predicted number of MIDs initiated per petrostate per year across
different levels of oil prices.12 Oil prices in around the median value
of \$40 per barrel are associated with .017 \[95% CI: .013−.021\] MIDs
initiated per petrostate per year, while those one standard deviation
below (\$10/barrel) are associated with .025 \[.016−.034\] compared to
.012 \[.007−.016\] at \$70 and .008 \[.003−.013\] at \$100. Moreover, as
Table A6 shows, the effect of oil prices does not hold for conflicts in
which the petrostate was on the receiving side of the conflict. This
placebo test offers greater confidence in the main results, as they
suggest that oil prices have a distinctive effect on petrostates'
behavior that does not hold for non-petrostates. Table A7, in turn,
suggests [that the relationship is **stronger** among petrostates that
produce **more oil**]{.underline}, whose revenues one would expect to be
more affected by changes in oil prices.13

Notably, [our results differ from]{.underline} those of
**[Hendrix]{.underline}** (2017, 584), who finds that lower oil prices
are not associated with greater petrostate conflict initiation. Several
differences between the two analyses can likely explain this. The first
is that [[our data extend to **2014**, rather than]{.mark}
**[2001]{.mark}**]{.underline}. The second is that [Hendrix uses a
time-varying measure of petrostate status]{.underline}, such that
countries drop in and out of that classification from year-to-year.
However, as we discussed previously, [using a time-varying measure risks
making inclusion into the sample **endogenous** to oil prices, as oil
income rises when the price increases]{.underline}. Indeed, when we
limit our sample to the pre-2002 period or use a time-varying measure of
petrostate status, our results, like those presented in Hendrix's Table
2, are weaker and in some cases not statistically distinguishable from
zero (see Table A12).

[The [results are **robust** to **different model
specifications**]{.mark}]{.underline}, as shown in the online appendix.
[[These include]{.underline}]{.mark} including [[**more control**
variables]{.underline}]{.mark} (Table A2); [using net export revenues of
at least 10% GDP]{.underline} or oil rents at 20% of GDP [as the
[sampling thresholds]{.mark}]{.underline} (Tables A3--A4); [including
country-decade fixed effects to account for]{.underline}
country-specific [time heterogeneity]{.underline} (Table A5); clustering
standard errors by year (Table A11); [[and]{.underline}]{.mark},
following Jang and Smith (2021), [controlling]{.underline} for [[an
Iran-Iraq]{.mark} War [dummy variable]{.mark}]{.underline} (Tables
A17-A18).

As a supplementary test to explore the effects of cross-national
variation in how constrained or abundant each petrostate's oil revenue
is, in Table A19, we replace oil prices with each country's
reserve-to-production (R/P) ratio, which measures the number of years'
worth of oil reserves the country has. Like oil prices, the R/P ratio
allows us to capture the degree to which a petrostate faces structural
constraints on its oil revenue. The results show that [countries with a
higher R/P ratio initiate fewer MIDs, **on average**, than those with a
lower R/P ratio]{.underline}. This is consistent with the main findings,
as it suggests that [petrostates facing constraints on their oil revenue
are **more likely** to initiate conflict]{.underline}.

Taken together, [[these]{.mark} results [offer **greater support**
for]{.mark} the Battling over a Smaller Market, Diversionary Conflict,
and **[Signaling Strength]{.mark}**]{.underline} explanations
[[than]{.underline}]{.mark} for the
**[[Emboldenment]{.underline}]{.mark}** mechanism. Across model
specifications, oil prices reduce the number of MIDs that petrostates
initiate. Moreover, Figure 2 shows how the predicted number of
petrostate-initiated MIDs across oil prices varies with or without the
presence of a revolutionary government. As the results show, high oil
prices do not increase petrostate conflict initiation even in the

#### That's especially true during the [green transition]{.underline}\-\--revenue loss makes [preemptive conquest]{.underline} likely.

**Blankenship 24**, PhD, Assistant Professor of Political Science at the

Conclusion: Implications for the Energy Transition

This article set out to understand the degree to which the foreign
policies of major oil-producers---and in particular their propensity to
initiate conflict---are shaped by variation in oil prices. The [findings
are more consistent with petrostates **initiating
conflict**]{.underline} more when [oil prices are **low**]{.underline},
[than with petrostates being **emboldened**]{.underline} by oil prices
and going on the offensive due to being flush with oil revenue. The key
implication of the findings, then, is that [[declining oil prices are
**not**]{.underline}]{.mark} necessarily [[**good news**
for]{.underline}]{.mark} "**[[petro-aggression]{.underline}]{.mark}**."
Previous periods of low oil prices have not been periods of petrostate
passivity; if anything, [petrostates have initiated more conflict
when]{.underline} oil [prices decline]{.underline}.

Our findings speak to the broader literature on "petro-aggression." As
described in the introduction, scholars disagree as to whether
petrostates are more aggressively overall, and are if anything even more
divided over whether petrostates fight each other over oil. While our
findings do not focus on petrostates' overall levels of aggression, they
suggest that petrostates are, in general, more likely to be aggressive
when oil prices are lower. Moreover, the findings may help to begin
bridging the gap between proponents and skeptics of what Meierding
(2016) calls the "oil wars hypothesis." Scholars like Meierding (2016,
2020), Jang and Smith (2021), and Ashford (2022) point out that
[[would-be conquerors face]{.mark} serious i[mpediments to]{.mark}
seeking to [seize]{.mark} and profit from [the]{.mark} oil [resources of
their neighbors]{.mark},]{.underline} including the costs of occupation
and the risk of international reprisals. Our findings suggest that while
these arguments may very well be correct, [[these constraints might be
**less likely**]{.mark} to **discourage** such attempts
[during]{.mark}]{.underline} periods of **[[low]{.underline}]{.mark}**
oil **[[prices]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

A natural question arises as to the degree to which these findings are
applicable to the energy transition that is likely to put downward
pressure on oil revenue in the coming years (see e.g. Van de Graaf 2018;
Bordoff 2020). The key ways in which declining revenue during the energy
transition is likely to differ from previous oil busts are twofold. The
first is that the energy transition's effects on oil revenue may not
necessarily be through the mechanism of prices if supply declines at a
similar rate as demand. Instead, it is more likely to be through the
volume of oil sold. Second, price fluctuations are difficult to predict
but temporary; petrostates could reasonably expect revenue to rebound,
as global demand for oil continued to climb. By contrast, slowing and
ultimately declining demand for oil is likely to be a gradual, long-term
process which petrostates can anticipate with some degree of certainty.
Our findings thus cannot necessarily be interpreted as predictions about
petrostates behavior during the energy transition, as petrostates may be
able to proactively adapt by, for example, diversifying their economies.

However, [this proactive adaptation could also include **conflict
initiation**]{.underline} based on mechanisms similar to those described
in this paper. But whereas we find that petrostates initiate conflict
after oil prices decline, [having [knowledge of]{.mark} their
[**impending decline** may lead petrostates to initiate]{.mark} in the
**[early]{.mark}** days of the transition. [States facing]{.mark} the
prospect of]{.underline} a [**[permanent decline]{.mark}** may [face
a]{.mark}]{.underline} "**[[use it or lose it]{.underline}]{.mark}**"
**[[incentive]{.underline}]{.mark}**---to try [to take advantage of [a
**closing window**]{.mark} of opportunity [to take advantage of their
position before it's too late]{.mark}]{.underline}, whether [[by
engaging in]{.mark} **coercion** or **[preventive war]{.mark}** against
rivals or]{.underline} trying to engage in [**conquest** to]{.underline}
try and **[stave off]{.underline}** their **[decline]{.underline}**
(Gilpin 1981; Van Evera 1999; Copeland 2000). Thus, [petrostates may
face the **worst** of both worlds]{.underline}---**[declining
revenue]{.underline}**, [and knowledge]{.underline} that [they
may]{.underline} [have **little to lose** since the future]{.underline}
may be even more disadvantageous. Somewhat counterintuitively, then,
[the [incentives for "**oil grabs**" may]{.mark} be **[highest]{.mark}**
during the **[earlier]{.mark}**]{.underline}, rather than later,
[**phase** of the energy transition.]{.underline}

#### Oil revenue [deepens interdependence]{.underline} and fosters [peace]{.underline}.

**Jang 21**, \*doctoral candidate in political science at the University
of Florida; \*\*associate professor of political science at the
University of Florida. (\*Hye Ryeon and \*\*Benjamin Smith, 2021, "Pax
Petrolica? Rethinking the Oil-Interstate War Linkage," *Security

Pax Petrolica: Theorizing an Oil Peace

Where a resource curse consensus in the domestic arena once existed, a
dissenting scholarly community has emerged. This group of heterodox
scholars has produced [[research]{.underline}]{.mark} that
[[shows]{.underline}]{.mark} either conditionally or wholly [[**positive
effects** for **oil wealth** on]{.underline}]{.mark} development,12
state capacity,13 and democracy.14 As we theorize in this section and
demonstrate empirically in the next, there are good theoretical reasons
to believe it may also have conditionally or direct salutary
international effects. Our theory of an oil
**[[peace]{.underline}]{.mark}** rests on three foundations: the
commercial peace thesis, the costs of war for oil producers, and the
dampening impact of oil wealth on the incentives for diversionary
foreign conflicts.15

International Costs of War

We start by observing that [[countries dependent on]{.underline}]{.mark}
the [revenues]{.underline} derived [from]{.underline} their
[[oil]{.underline}]{.mark} and gas sectors [[rely
on]{.underline}]{.mark} the willingness of [[importing countries to
buy]{.mark} their [fossil fuels]{.mark}]{.underline}, and usually on
foreign investors for the ability to deliver it to buyers. First, and
put simply, oil is commercial trade, a global network, the purchasing
and selling of a commodity that happens to be disproportionately
important to the world economy. [Because]{.underline} it is so important
to exporting countries' revenues, and [[because losing those revenues
would be]{.underline}]{.mark} so
**[[catastrophic]{.underline}]{.mark}**, we suggest that [[it is likely
to encourage]{.underline}]{.mark} more
**[[pacific]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[not]{.underline}** more
**[belligerent]{.underline}**, international
[[behavior]{.underline}]{.mark}. Subsequently, the oil peace falls into
the broader framework of the commercial.16 [Due to the peace-promoting
impact of]{.underline} both [trade dependence and]{.underline} of the
[diffusion of]{.underline} peaceful [conflict resolutions
through]{.underline} interstate [trade networks, greater economic
integration can lead states to **set aside**]{.underline} their
proclivities to choose **[war]{.underline}** over statecraft. [The
benefits of peace are a **powerful**]{.underline} positive
[**incentive** for pacific behavior on]{.underline} the part of
[oil-producing states]{.underline}. We suggest that [[commercial]{.mark}

**[[sway]{.underline}]{.mark}** over [**[oil-exporting

[exporter**s**]{.underline}. As Erik Gartzke and Oliver Westerwinter
suggest, "for trade to act as a barrier to conflict, the commercial
losses anticipated from fighting must be large relative to the overall

simply too difficult to achieve complete coordination, nearly all the

states. In short, we find strong reason to expect that, at the
international-system level, constraints on bellicosity should lead
rulers in oil-producing countries to be less inclined toward initiating
conflicts.

Domestic Substitution: Side Payments and Not Scapegoats

At the domestic level, [[there are]{.underline}]{.mark} equally
[[**compelling reasons** to expect an **oil
peace**]{.underline}]{.mark}. These reasons stem from the logic of the
domestic diversion or "scapegoat" framework for understanding the
international determinants of conflict initiation. That logic suggests
that, [[facing]{.mark} popular [dissent]{.mark} or disapproval at home,
[leaders]{.mark} might [engage in **military action
abroad**]{.mark}]{.underline} (especially territorial conflicts) as a
means of trying [[to **catalyze** a **rally-around-the-flag
effect**]{.mark} to bolster their **domestic positions**.]{.underline}19
This line of scholarship often suggests that [diversionary]{.underline}
military [adventurism seeks to **distract** from **economic
underperformance**]{.underline}. Conversely, we might expect that
**[high]{.underline}**er [**levels of oil income** might enable leaders
to **circumvent**]{.underline} political [**dissent** by using oil
revenues for social policy increases]{.underline} or other kinds of side
payments. These reasons also reinforce the international system-level
peace effect, leading us, overall, to expect oil producers to be less
bellicose than nonoil producers.

[[Where **other**]{.mark} leader**[s]{.mark}**]{.underline} might
[[initiate conflicts]{.underline}]{.mark} abroad [[to
**distract**]{.mark} **opposition**]{.underline} at home [[from
**economic**]{.underline}]{.mark} or political
**[[problems]{.underline}]{.mark}** (diversionary conflict initiation),
[rulers in [oil-rich states]{.mark} could [**buy domestic peace** by
allocating **resource rents** to **public goods**]{.mark}
**provision**]{.underline} or widely distributed club goods. Cristina
Bodea, Masaaki Higashijima, and Raju Jan Singh find that [this dynamic
exists in the context of **blunting** the likelihood of **civil
conflict**]{.underline}---**[[public spending
minimizes]{.underline}]{.mark}** the risk of civil
**[[wars]{.underline}]{.mark}**.20 Applying that logic of oil cushioning
spending, we postulate it to be related to more peaceful international
behavior as well. As such, we expect that:

> Hypothesis 1a (H1a). If a country is a petrostate (oil exports are
> greater than 10% of total exports or greater than 10% of gross
> domestic product (GDP)), then it is less likely to initiate MIDs.
>
> Hypothesis 1b (H1b). The more oil/gas income a country receives each
> year, the less likely it is to initiate MIDs.21

#### Oil exporters are less aggressive\-\--[trust the data]{.underline}. 

**Jang 21**, \*Hye Ryeon, doctoral candidate in political science at the
University of Florida; \*\*Benjamin Smith, associate professor of
political science at the University of Florida. (2021, "Pax Petrolica?

Discussion and Conclusion

Based on this elaborated analysis, it is possible to advance some
preliminary conclusions. First, [to the extent]{.underline} that [oil
wealth has a]{.underline} consistent [effect]{.underline}, [the evidence
suggests it is]{.underline} generally [a **peaceful** one]{.underline}.
All else equal, [[oil-producing countries tend to be **less
aggressive**]{.underline}]{.mark} toward their neighbors than non-oil
producers once we account for the statistical outlier that is the
Iran--Iraq War. [[This]{.mark} relationship [holds across **multiple
measurement**]{.mark}]{.underline} choice**[[s and
model]{.underline}]{.mark}** specification**[[s]{.underline}]{.mark}**.
[[Even revolutionary]{.mark} oil-producing [countries are no more
bellicose]{.mark}]{.underline} than revolutionary non-oil-producing
countries. When we account for the Iran--Iraq War, oil prices also,
whether alone or interacted with oil wealth and radical leadership, have
at best an inconsistent impact on the risk of interstate conflict.

In short, [[the relationships we find]{.mark} here
[suggest]{.mark}]{.underline} the possibility of [[an **oil
peace**]{.underline}]{.mark}. What remains open to investigation are the
mechanisms that might link oil wealth to more peaceful international
behavior. Are they primarily domestic or systemic? And which
implications of oil wealth at each level appear to be steering
substantial oil producers toward more pacific international roles? To
the extent that oil-producing countries are more peaceful than non-oil
producers, it may well suggest a need to "normalize" oil producers as
ones whose behavior is explained by the commercial peace thesis like
that of producers of other commodities.43 These questions of causal
process are an obvious next step for scholarship seeking to reconcile
the inconsistency of the oil--interstate war thesis with the suggestive
evidence for an oil peace.

Methodologically, our findings here suggest several things. First, [[it
is]{.mark} past [time to bring **measurement**]{.mark} choices [in the
**oil**]{.mark}-interstate [**war** research agenda **up to date**. The
data we analyze]{.mark} here for oil and gas income per capita, which is
publicly archived, [employs the **best available measure** of oil
abundance and]{.mark}]{.underline} its original form [[extends from
**1932** to **2014**]{.underline}]{.mark}.44 For scholars seeking to
complement that with a ratio measure of dependence, best practices in
the resource curse research program most often now use oil/gas income as
a share of GDP rather than only export revenues. Moreover, we lack any
good empirical or theoretical reasons to establish categorical
boundaries between what is a "petrostate" and what is not, and unless
and until we do, it makes more sense to use continuous data. Second,
unless there are solid reasons to dismiss system-level causal factors in
our statistical models (and there are certainly not at present---on the
contrary, nearly all current studies of war onset find multiple
important determinants at this level of analysis), scholars should
employ random-effects estimators.

**Smith 21**, \*PhD, Chair of the Department of Political Science,
University of Florida. \*\*PhD, Assistant Professor in the Department of

\*\*David Waldner, "Rethinking the Resource Curse," p. 72-75, Cambridge
