### UQ\-\--Warming Turns\-\--2AC

#### Warming decimates Canada's economy.

**Willcott and Cleary 22**, \*PhD Candidate in Finance at Queen's
University in Ontario, \*\*Sean, PhD in finance from the University of
Toronto, Professor of Finance at Queen's University. (Neal, May 18,
2022, "Canada faces huge physical costs from climate change, making net
zero a great investment," Queen's Gazette,
https://www.queensu.ca/gazette/stories/canada-faces-huge-physical-costs-climate-change-making-net-zero-great-investment)

There has been a lot of discussion in Canada lately about the financial
costs of achieving the country's climate targets. And rightly so. [[The
situation is **urgent**]{.underline}]{.mark} and we need to act now.

Fighting climate change will require a concerted effort, affecting all
sectors of the economy. And while there will be great economic
opportunity and lots of new jobs in the green economy, there will be
considerable disruptions in the workforce, major economic challenges and
significant capital investment required.

However, we in the finance business like to look at both sides of the
ledger. And [when one considers the damage]{.underline} to the Canadian
economy [we can expect from [fires, floods, melting ice
caps]{.mark}]{.underline} and loss of biodiversity due to climate
change, the investment in greenhouse gas reductions starts to look very
worthwhile indeed.

Climate change impacts economic prosperity

In a new study we recently published with the Institute for Sustainable
Finance, we posit that economic value is sacrificed every day that
action is not taken to mitigate the economic and ecological risks posed
by climate change. Existing economic models agree that losses are
unavoidable without change and investment. But questions remained
regarding how much value will be lost and how quickly.

Our study modelled the physical risk to Canada, or how much capital
output might be lost, over various warming scenarios between now and the
end of the century. We found that [under a business-as-usual
scenario]{.underline}, with no new international greenhouse gas
mitigation measures taken, allowing the climate to warm 5 C by 2100,
[[the cumulative cost]{.mark} to Canada [would be **\$5.5
trillion**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

That's a big number. And it's a lot higher than the damage we would see
[under a scenario [where]{.mark} global [warming is kept to 2
C]{.mark}]{.underline}, which [[we estimate]{.mark} to be [around
**\$2.8 trillion**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Of course, this is just the financial cost and [does not take into
account the suffering of those who will lose livelihoods, homes and
businesses]{.underline}, or even their lives, due to climate-related
disasters.

Our study further reveals that the [[associated costs of physical damage
are **larger** than the investments required to reduce
g]{.mark}reen[h]{.mark}ouse [g]{.mark}as
emission[s]{.mark}]{.underline}. In fact, the difference is up to \$45.4
billion larger than the required investment. And this doesn't even
consider the potential economic benefits of transitioning to a
low-carbon economy.

Yes, it is true that Canada can't fight climate change on its own, and
that it's a global effort. But the incentive is clear for a rich,
developed, industrialized country like Canada to take a global
leadership role and meet our own net-zero targets.

Quick action is crucial

There is also a big incentive to act now, as we will face some
inflection points in the coming decades that will make the challenge
considerably more difficult.

Our study found that the costs of climate change damage are expected to
grow gradually until 2050, around which time there is a sharp increase
under all scenarios. By 2070 there is an exponential increase in
damages. These dates correspond to two of the significant target dates
for achieving net zero noted by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate
Change in its reports.

Despite the recent increased attention to addressing climate change,
progress has been too slow. It is becoming clear that we are not on
pace to limit the global temperature increase to 1.5 C above
pre-industrial levels by 2100. Estimates from the IPCC and others
suggest that with current progress, and if the world meets its existing
commitments, we are more on pace for a 3 C warming scenario. There is a
very real risk that warming will be higher still.

This is all bad news for [[Canada]{.underline}]{.mark}, which [[is
**highly susceptible** to the impacts]{.mark}]{.underline} of climate
change [[and is warming at **twice the rate** of the global
average]{.underline}]{.mark}. But until now, we haven't had an effective
assessment of the physical risks and the potential capital cost to
Canada.

[We have much to lose]{.underline}. And it should now be clear that
tackling climate change more than pays for itself in terms of avoided
physical damage alone.![The
Conversation](media/image3.gif){width="8.333333333333333e-3in"
height="8.333333333333333e-3in"}

#### The costs compound over time and reducing emissions solves.

**Beugin and Sawyer 22**, \*Executive Vice President of the Canadian
Climate Institute, master's degree in resource management from Simon
Fraser University, \*\*Dave, Principal Economist at the Canadian Climate
Institute, Executive in Residence at the University of Ottawa. (Dale,
October 28, 2022, "The GDP costs of climate change for Canada," Canadian
Climate Institute,
https://climateinstitute.ca/the-gdp-costs-of-climate-change-for-canada/)

Our new research shows that [the [macroeconomic
costs]{.mark}]{.underline} of climate change [[are **already
big**]{.mark} and are poised to get **even larger**]{.underline} over
time. It's these cumulative effects that make it particularly hard to
get a handle on the full scale of potential damages. [We aren't talking
about periodic shocks]{.underline} that undermine growth in any one
year: [[climate change will be a drag]{.mark} on growth
[in every year]{.mark}]{.underline}. And, unless we take policy action,
those costs are going to add-up in a serious way. 

Modelling the costs of climate change

Damage Control is the culmination of the Canadian Climate Institute's
Costs of Climate Change series, a multi-year modelling and research
project that aims to better understand and quantify the potential costs
of a changing climate in Canada. The analysis integrates two global
greenhouse gas emissions scenarios, downscaled projections from seven
different global climate models, and two adaptation scenarios in a
macroeconomic model of Canada's economy. All told, our analysis
simulates 84 scenarios to bound a wide range of uncertainty. 

The upshot? [The costs of climate change for Canada are already
big]{.underline}---and they are growing. 

Climate damages mean lower GDP 

Those economic damages aren't in some far-off future. Our analysis
suggests that [[by 2025]{.underline}]{.mark}, the previous 10 years of
climate change will have shaved \$25 billion off of national GDP. In
other words, [[the]{.mark} Canadian econ[omy will be **\$25
billion**]{.mark} dollars [smaller]{.mark} than it would have been if
global warming had stabilized in 2015]{.underline}.The economy still
grows at a fast clip, but the cumulative drag adds up.  

That's a big number. To break it down, \$25 billion [[represents **half
a year** of growth]{.mark} in the Canadian economy]{.underline}, \$630
per person of lost national income, is equivalent to more than twice the
cost of the November 2021 floods that ravaged British Columbia, and is
12 times larger than all insured losses from weather-related disasters
in Canada in 2021. 

It's also just the beginning. Critically \$25 billion dollars isn't just
the result of climate shocks---storms, floods, fires, and more---in
2025. It represents the accumulation of impacts from each subsequent
year. Our modelling simulation starts in 2015. Each year, climate
impacts mean that GDP is lower than it would have otherwise been, and it
grows a little bit more slowly than it otherwise would have. 

That means that [the [costs]{.mark}]{.underline} of climate change
[effectively [**compound** over time]{.mark}]{.underline}. The drag on
the economy from climate impacts will accumulate even more in the
longer-term, as the figure below illustrates. [By 2030, GDP will be \$35
billion lower]{.underline} than it would have otherwise been---[[by
2055, it will be]{.underline}]{.mark} \$80 to [[\$**103 billion
lower**]{.underline}]{.mark}.  

The true costs of climate change for Canadians will be even larger

The [GDP]{.underline} impacts of a changing climate in Damage
Control convey the urgency of the climate crisis for Canada. Yet even
these large [impacts tell only part of the story.]{.underline} 

GDP masks some of the more acute impacts. It's not just the total
damages to the economy that matters, it's also who pays for those
damages. In our modelling, [governments **backstop** the impacts of
climate change]{.underline} by helping to rebuild damaged infrastructure
and assets, paying for increased healthcare costs, and fixing damages
from weather-related disasters. [That [diverts capital from activities
that would]{.mark} actually [drive **additional
growth**]{.mark}]{.underline}, rather than rebuilding what we already
have. But it also means that taxpayers ultimately bear the brunt of
climate impacts. 

Our analysis finds that [all households will lose income]{.underline},
and low-income households will suffer the most. Low-income households
could see income losses of 12 per cent in a low-emissions scenario and
23 per cent in a high-emissions scenario by the end of the century.  

Furthermore, our results only represent the "tip of the iceberg" when it
comes to the full range of effects to Canada's economy and society from
a changing climate. There remain many below-the-waterline risks, where
climate change will likely generate even greater economic impacts, but
that have too much uncertainty and complexity to quantify at this
stage. 

The long-term impacts of climate change aren't yet written in stone

The economic stakes of climate change for Canada are high. Yet our
analysis is fundamentally a warning, not a prediction: action now can
still avert the highest costs. 

Adaption---action that reduces the negative impacts of climate change
like building seawalls and strengthening bridges and hydro lines---pays
off big. Every dollar spent on adaptation measures saves \$13-\$15,
including both direct and indirect economy-wide benefits. Taking
proactive adaptation measures cuts climate costs in half. That means
that Canada's forthcoming National Adaptation Strategy is a hugely
important policy opportunity for Canada to begin the steps to prepare
for climate change and seize those very large potential savings.  

At the same time, [[limiting]{.mark} further
[warming]{.mark}]{.underline}, while also adapting to the warming
already baked in, [[pays off **bigger**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Combining
proactive adaptation in Canada with global progress in [[reducing
emissions]{.underline}]{.mark} [[could reduce the
costs]{.underline}]{.mark} of climate change for Canada [[by
**three-quarters**]{.underline}]{.mark}. \
Canada  is making progress. But the lessons from Damage Control are
clear: [[maximizing economic growth]{.underline}]{.mark}---and the
prosperity for Canadians that comes with it---[[requires taking climate
policy]{.underline}]{.mark} (both adaptation and mitigation) much more
**[[seriously]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

#### Disasters everyday cost hundreds of millions of dollars.

**Matakala 24**, master's in political science from the University of
the Witwatersrand in South Africa, Climate Justice Campaigner for Oxfam
Canada. (Mwangla, October 24, 2024, "The True Cost of Climate Inaction
in Canada," Oxfam Canada,
https://www.oxfam.ca/story/the-true-cost-of-climate-inaction-in-canada/)

Climate Inaction Costs Lives... and Billions 

We're [[**already** feeling the effects]{.mark}]{.underline} of climate
inactions [[across Canada]{.underline}]{.mark}. [The financial toll
from]{.underline} [this year's two biggest]{.underline} extreme weather
[disasters has already surpassed \$1.8 billion]{.underline} in insured
losses alone. [[A **single afternoon** of heavy
rain]{.underline}]{.mark} in Toronto this July [[caused flooding
that]{.underline}]{.mark} winded up
[[cost]{.underline}]{.mark}ing [almost **[\$1
billion]{.mark}**]{.underline}, while the
[[wildfire]{.underline}]{.mark} that ravaged parts of Jasper, the ninth
costliest natural disaster in Canada's history, [[will cost at
least \$880 million]{.underline}]{.mark} in insured losses. 

[These numbers only account for insured losses]{.underline}. [The real
cost]{.underline} of climate disasters -- to marginalized indigenous
communities, people's livelihoods, and ecosystems -- [is
**incalculable**]{.underline}. And, as always, those suffering the most
from climate impacts are people living in poverty, particularly women
and children, who have contributed the least to the crisis. Yet, despite
these devastating losses, funding provided to date to help communities
adapt to climate impacts falls dangerously short. The [[U]{.mark}nited
[N]{.mark}ations]{.underline} Environment Programme (UNEP) [[estimates
that **\$387 billion** a year is required for climate adaptation
efforts]{.underline}]{.mark}, but current funding is nowhere near that
level. 

## IL

### I/L\-\--High Prices\-\--War\-\--AT: Oil DA\-\--2AC

#### High prices cause war and instability: petrol-aggression.

**Ashford 22**, Adjunct Professor of Security Studies at Georgetown
University, Ph.D., Foreign Affairs, University of Virginia, M.A.,
International Affairs, American University, B.A., Foreign Affairs,
University of Virginia (Emma Ashford, June 19, 2022, "The Problem With
Being a Petrostate," Foreign Policy,
https://foreignpolicy.com/2022/06/19/petrostates-oil-production-weapon-foreign-policy-war-economy/)

Cicero, perhaps apocryphally, is said to have written that ["endless
money forms the sinews of war." **War** and **wealth** are intrinsically
**linked**]{.underline}. A nation's ability to wage war is inherently
dependent on its ability to raise and fund armed forces.

Oil-wealthy states---defined here as those that earn more than \$1,000
per capita in oil and natural gas revenues each year---hold vast, easily
obtained wealth that makes them almost uniquely capable of waging war
without sacrificing domestic priorities. Due to high levels of income
from oil and gas production, they can afford to build up military
capacity, buy weapons, increase military salaries, or engage in research
and development, all without substantial budgetary trade-offs. To put it
another way: If they wish, [[leaders in **oil**]{.mark}-wealthy
**[states]{.mark}** can [have guns **and** butter]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Oil-wealthy states are a diverse bunch, [[including **Russia**]{.mark},
small and large **[Mid]{.mark}**dle **[East]{.mark}**ern
[states]{.mark}]{.underline} (Qatar, Iran), several wealthy democracies
(Norway, Australia), [[and]{.mark} various]{.underline} underdeveloped
[[**African** and]{.mark} Central [**Asian**
nations]{.mark}]{.underline} (Libya, Equatorial Guinea, Turkmenistan).
Yet although they face a diverse set of national security challenges,
[most **share** common **trends** in their military **expenditures**:
[As oil **prices rise**, so does military **spending**. When]{.mark} oil
**[prices crash]{.mark}**, military [spending]{.mark} eventually
**[drops]{.mark}**. Cross-national **[stat]{.mark}**istical
analysi[**s** confirm]{.mark}]{.underline}s that [oil-wealthy states
**spend more**]{.underline} on their militaries on an absolute basis,
[regardless of **regime type** or major-**power status**.]{.underline}

Likewise, data on arms sales shows a strong correlation between oil
wealth and weapons purchases. According to a metric from the Stockholm
International Peace Research Institute, although wealthy oil states are
a relatively small proportion of all states globally, they have been
well represented among top arms importers, both during the Cold War and
after. Global arms sales have largely risen in line with oil prices in
recent decades.

Of course, there are other ways to transform money into military
capacity, notably in the research and development of advanced
technologies. The most controversial investment in technological
research is the development of a nuclear weapons program. Data on
nuclear programs and nuclear latency reveals that [[oil]{.mark}
**wealth** [is]{.mark} strongly [correlated with]{.mark}]{.underline}
nuclear development, with oil-wealthy states on average more likely to
have [an [active **nuclear**]{.mark} program or]{.underline} to have
[the **latent** nuclear **[capacity]{.mark}**]{.underline} necessary to
build one quickly.

Interestingly, oil wealth is not correlated with higher spending as a
percentage of government expenditure. In plain English, this means that
oil-rich and oil-poor states appear to spend comparable chunks of their
overall budgets on their militaries. If the state budget is a pie, then
oil-wealthy states simply have a much larger pie, with the government
spending more overall.

And while oil-wealthy states may spend more on their militaries, that
doesn't necessarily mean they have more combat capability to show for
it. Arms must be chosen wisely to meet key threats, and troops must be
trained on new weapon systems to use them effectively. Spending a lot on
prestige weapon systems---fighter jets or the latest in high-tech
gadgets---may look impressive but yield little military advantage.

Politically difficult changes in strategy, doctrine, or force structure
may be necessary to improve military effectiveness, even with higher
levels of spending. This is particularly the case in countries with high
levels of corruption and underdeveloped institutions---maladies common
in oil-wealthy states. Indeed, we've seen many of these flaws on public
display in recent months in Russia's invasion of Ukraine. Despite
billions poured into Russia's military modernization over the last
decade, the military has performed abysmally.

Even well-implemented military spending doesn't always yield battlefield
effectiveness. For authoritarian regimes, battlefield effectiveness may
be impeded by other regime priorities such as coup-proofing. Consider
Saddam Hussein's Iraq, where efforts at coup-proofing against his army
resulted in poor performance and near disaster during the early years of
the Iran-Iraq War in the 1980s. Any army that prioritizes domestic
policing and repression is not necessarily an effective fighting force
against peer competitors.

As in any state, therefore, military expenditure must be coupled with
effective management and strategic doctrine to produce a capable force.
What military expenditure does offer, mainly, is potential---the
potential to substantially improve military capability if it is spent
wisely.

The more difficult question is whether increased military expenditure
itself makes a state more likely to initiate conflicts. One could argue
that oil wealth doesn't cause war but merely facilitates it.

Yet [**[excessive]{.mark}** military [**spending** could easily
increase]{.mark} the risk of **[conflict]{.mark}**]{.underline}. The
connection is simple: [Having [a]{.mark} well-equipped and
**well-[funded]{.mark}** [military gives leaders]{.mark} **more
[confidence]{.mark}** in their ability [to win]{.mark}]{.underline}.
These [leaders can assume]{.underline}, perhaps wrongly, that [they have
**assembled** a superior fighting force]{.underline}. Baathist Iraq---a
particularly belligerent petrostate---provides an illustration of this:
Saddam's conviction in his own military genius, his faith in new
armaments, and his belief that his troops performed well (despite all
evidence) often led him to conflicts he could not win. Libya's Muammar
al-Qaddafi made similar mistakes.

This offers one potential explanation for [[oil]{.mark}-wealthy
[states']{.mark}]{.underline} tendency toward conflict: Their
[[**overconfident** leaders]{.mark} mistake **high** military
**spending** for actual capability]{.underline}. It also suggests an
unfortunate corollary: [Oil-wealthy states]{.underline} not only [[are
**more likely** to start wars]{.underline}]{.mark} but may be more
likely to lose them. Oil wealth may even cushion the negative impact of
losing a war, [providing ways [to **buy off**]{.mark}]{.underline}
segments of [[society and]{.mark} allowing a leader to **[maintain
power]{.mark}** despite]{.underline} repeated **[conflict]{.underline}**
losses.

###  1AR\-\--I/L\-\--High Prices\-\--Top

#### High prices are bad: they boost [war chests]{.underline} which causes [overconfidence]{.underline} in petrostates and [lines the pockets]{.underline} of doves\-\--that's Ashford. It's [empirically proven]{.underline} in Russia and Middle East states [regional wars]{.underline}.

#### This is empirical law\-\--the most comprehensive data agrees. 

**Hendrix 17**, International Studies Professor at the University of
Denver \<3 (Cullen S. Hendrix, 2017, "Oil prices and interstate
conflict," Conflict Management and Peace Science, Vol. 34, No. 6, pp.
575--596, University of Kansas Libraries, SAGE Journals)

Data, estimation, and results

[[This **study** builds on]{.underline}]{.mark} replications of
**[Colgan]{.underline}** (2010) [and **Weeks**]{.underline} (2012). The
former is a monadic8 analysis of the effects [of oil exporter
status]{.underline} on MIDs. The latter is a directeddyadic9 analysis of
MID initiation. The Colgan replication is conducted on a sample
comprising 153 states for the period 1947--2001; coverage of the
variables restricts the analysis to 6014 country-years. The Weeks
replication is conducted on a sample of 25,953 directed dyads for the
period 1947--1998; coverage of the variables restricts the analysis to a
maximum of [**[728,226]{.mark}** directed
[dyad-**years**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Although the Weeks study did not
originally investigate the effects of oil prices on dispute behavior, it
is a very highly cited and well-respected recent contribution to the
quantitative empirical literature on MID initiation.

For the Colgan reanalysis, the dependent variable is the country-year
count of MIDs. Behaviorally, MIDs range from relatively minor acts, such
as or including troop incursions into disputed territory and firing
warning shots, and attacks resulting in fatalities. [This is considered
[the most **comprehensive**]{.mark} and widely used **[data]{.mark}
source** [on]{.mark} interstate **[conflicts]{.mark}**]{.underline}.
Tests for two operationalizations are presented: total MIDs,
irrespective of which state instigated, and instigated MIDs, those in
which the country in question is the initiator (i.e. took the first
action). The mean for MIDs is 0.5, with at least one MID occurring in
roughly 25% of country-years, and with a maximum value of 26 (Iran in
1987). The mean for instigated MIDs is 0.24, with at least one MID
instigated in 10% of country-years, with a maximum of 23 (Iran in 1987).
For the Weeks reanalysis, the dependent variable is whether or not
country A initiated a MID against country B in a given year. Initiated
MIDs are quite rare, with initiated MIDs occurring in 0.18% of directed
dyad-years.

The main independent variable is the real price of oil per barrel (West
Texas Intermediate, or WTI) in constant 2008 dollars; data are from
Hamilton (2009).10 WTI, along with Brent Blend and Dubai/Oman, is one of
the three major benchmarks against which crude contracts are pegged.11
Table 1 presents prices by decade for the period under study
(1947--2001). Oil prices ranged from a low of US\$17.54 per barrel in
1947 to a high of US\$96.72 per barrel in 1980. Average prices were
highest in the 1980s, when oil traded at prices 157% higher than the
average of the 1960s. The relative standard deviation (RSD, s/m)
indicates that while prices were higher on average in the 1980s, prices
were more volatile in the 1970s, the decade characterized by two OPEC
embargos. This stands in contrast to the 1950s and 1960s, a period
characterized by comparatively low prices and very low price volatility.

To test for conditional effects, the price variable is interacted with
several variables drawn from Colgan (2010). Petrostate is a dummy coding
that takes a value of one if revenues from net oil exports constitute at
least 10% of GDP, and zero otherwise. Petrostates account for 11.6% of
country-years and 13% of directed dyad-years in the samples, with 11
states meeting this threshold for the entire time period.12

Revolutionary leader is a dummy coding for whether the head of state was
a revolutionary leader or not. Colgan's definition of a revolutionary
leader has two components. The first component involves the means by
which the ruler comes in to office: ''First, has the individual leader
used armed force against his own state at any time prior to coming to
office as an integral part of coming to national influence, and
ultimately, state leadership? Second, were there mass demonstrations or
uprisings, violent or non-violent, that were instrumental in deciding
the outcome of the transition?'' (Colgan, 2012: 444--445). The second
component refers to the types of policies the leader implemented while
in office: ''Did the leader usher in a major change to the constitution?
Did the leader adopt communism or fascism as the official ideology of
the state/ruling party? Did the leader overhaul rules governing property
ownership?'' (Colgan, 2012: 445). Revolutionary leaders are present in
13.8% of country-years in the sample, with Russia/Union of Soviet
Socialist Republics (82%), Cuba (81%), Albania (79%), Serbia/Yugoslavia
(78%), and the Democratic Republic of the Congo (76%) having the largest
proportion of time spent under revolutionary governments. For a complete
list of revolutionary leaders, see Colgan (2012).

Various controls are included as well. In the Colgan reanalysis, (ln)
population size and (ln) GDP per capita are included. Larger states have
engaged in more disputes, while the effect of GDP per capita is
theoretically ambiguous: wealthier states tend to have more resources to
invest in military capacity, but this would affect both their ability to
project force and their ability to deter attempts to use force against
them. Since major powers tend to be more involved in international
disputes than even their population size and level of development would
suggest, a control for major power status is included, as is a control
for democracy. While pairs of democracies are much less likely to fight
(Oneal and Russett, 1997), the theoretical effect of democracy in the
monadic sense is less well established. However, it is important to
include it as a control, since oil exporters tend to be more autocratic
than non-oil-exporting countries (Anderson and Ross, 2014; Ross, 2001).
Democracy is operationalized using the revised combined Polity score, a
21-point scale ranging from 10 (strong democracies) to 210 (strong
autocracies) (Marshall and Jaggers, 2009). Finally, controls for
temporal dependence---peace years, or a count of years since a country's
last MID, and its squared and cubed terms---are included as well.13 All
specifications include panel fixed effects and clustered standard
errors.14 All independent variables are lagged one time period to
address concerns about the endogeneity of prices to conflict behavior, a
relationship that will be probed further in robustness checks. Because
the dependent variable is a count variable, negative binomial and
Poisson regression are used.

In the Weeks reanalysis, the controls include indicator variables for a
variety of authoritarian regime types (machines, juntas, bosses and
strongmen; see Weeks (2012) for a description), measures of military
capabilities, derived from the Correlates of War operationalizations
thereof, for both states, major and minor power status, and peace years
and its squared and cubic terms.15 As the outcome is binary, logistic
regression is used. Specifications include either directed dyad random
effects or fixed effects as indicated.16 Random and fixed effects
represent two alternatives for addressing otherwise unmodeled attributes
of directed dyads that affect the baseline propensity for conflict
initiation.

Table 2 presents results for the Colgan reanalysis; the odd-numbered
models correspond to MID onsets and the even-numbered to instigated
MIDs. Interaction terms complicate interpretation of coefficients, since
levels of statistical significance are conditional on the mediating
variable taking on a value of zero (Braumoeller, 2004). The dummy
specifications of petrostate and revolutionary leadership, however,
simplify interpretation. The coefficient on oil price represents the
effect of oil prices on non-petrostate, non-revolutionary leader-led
countries, while the coefficients on the petrostate and revolutionary
leader represent the effect of these factors on MIDs and instigated MIDs
when oil prices are zero---the effect necessarily varies across observed
values of the interacted variable. To assess the impact of oil prices in
petrostates and under revolutionary leaders, one must calculate the
conditional slope as an additive function of the coefficients on oil
price and the interaction terms.

The results provide support for H1. [[**Higher** oil prices]{.mark} are
associated with an **[increase]{.mark}**d frequency of [**MID onsets**
in petrostates]{.mark}]{.underline}, but not in non-petrostates. All
things being equal, a [[one]{.mark} **standard
[deviation]{.mark}**]{.underline} (US\$18.60) [[increase in]{.mark} the
**[price]{.mark} of oil**]{.underline} per barrel from the sample mean
(US\$33.81) [[is]{.mark} associated with [a **13%** increase in]{.mark}
the frequency of **[MIDs]{.mark}**]{.underline} (model 2.1). Phrased in
Friedman's terms, [petrostates are roughly **30%** more **likely** to be
involved in **disputes** when oil prices are at US**\$60** per barrel
**than** when they are at US**\$20** per barrel]{.underline}. No such
relationship is found for non-petrostates, providing support for H4.
Confirming the results of Colgan's earlier analysis, however, the
confluence of petrostate status and revolutionary leadership is
associated with significantly more bellicose behavior, and this finding
is not price contingent: the petrostate 3 revolutionary leader
interaction term is positive and significant in all four specifications,
while the oil price 3 petrostate 3 revolutionary leader interaction term
is not statistically significant in all four specifications and the
confidence intervals for revolutionary-led petrostates are so large as
to be statistically indistinguishable from the other categories
(non-petrostates and petrostates). Given that revolutionary-led
petrostates are still petrostates, however, their dispute propensity
increases as prices increase, although the proportional effects are
smaller given the higher baseline incidence of MIDs.

The [monadic [**results** indicate]{.mark} that the
**[bellicosity]{.mark}** of petrostates]{.underline} relative to
non-petrostates [[is **price-contingent**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Figure 3
plots predicted counts of MIDs for revolutionary-led petrostates,
petrostates without revolutionary leaders, and non-petrostates across a
range of values for oil prices. Above US\$70 per barrel, petrostates are
significantly more dispute-prone than non-petrostates: although the
confidence intervals overlap, two-sample t-tests confirm that the
estimated counts of MIDs for petrotates and non-petrostates are
significant at and above US\$70 per barrel.17 Below US\$30 per barrel,
petrostates are less dispute-prone than non-petrostates, although the
finding is not statistically significant. Revolutionary-led petrostates
are the most dispute-prone across all oil prices, although the finding
is statistically insignificant above US\$70 per barrel.

#### High prices cause war: they [satiate opposition]{.underline} and [sanction spending]{.underline} for foreign aggression.

**Ashford 22**, Adjunct Professor of Security Studies at Georgetown
University, Ph.D., Foreign Affairs, University of Virginia, M.A.,
International Affairs, American University, B.A., Foreign Affairs,
University of Virginia (Emma Ashford, 2022, "Chapter 3: Resource Arms
Racing: Oil Wealth and Military Power," in *Oil, The State, And War: The
Foreign Policies of Petrostates*, pp. 55-88, Georgetown University
Press, University of Kansas Libraries, ILL)

Cause or Lubricant? Military Expenditure and Conflict

Perhaps a more challenging question is whether wealth and military
expenditure can itself make states more likely to initiate conflicts.
Certainly, there is a clear and compelling case to be made that [[oil
wealth **smooths**]{.mark} the conduct of [**war**, making it
**cheaper**]{.mark} and **easier** for petrostates [to sustain
**conflict**]{.mark}, pay [**troops**, and buy]{.mark}]{.underline}
replacement [**[arm]{.mark}**ament**[s]{.mark}**. Oil [wealth]{.mark}
undoubtedly **[lowers]{.mark}** the [**barriers** to conflict]{.mark} by
making it **more affordable** and **less costly** for
states]{.underline}, at least in monetary terms. But it is an altogether
different thing to argue that oil wealth actually makes states more
prone to start conflicts; this is the reason prior studies have focused
on other factors---such as the personality foibles of revolutionary
leaders---to explain why oil-rich states end up in wars so often.69 Is
oil wealth merely a lubricant for states already interested in war, or
is it an actual cause of those conflicts? Or to put it another way, does
oil wealth cause war, or does it merely facilitate it? 70

While this chapter has primarily focused on the facilitating effects of
oil wealth---in effect, increased military expenditure---it is worth
pointing out that even within purely rationalist models of conflict
initiation, excessive military spending could easily increase the risk
of conflict. The connection is simple: having a well-equipped and funded
military increases the attractiveness of military options to leaders,
leading them to be more confident of their ability to win a conflict. In
short, when deciding whether to enter a conflict, leaders weigh the
potential risks of losing against the possible benefits of winning.
Since waging (and losing) war is costly, states try to generate an
assessment of the likelihood of winning or losing before they enter the
conflict, considering such factors as available resources, public (or
private) support for a war, and perceptions about an opponent\'s
strength and resolve. Theoretically, this means wars should be rare. But
misperceptions and overconfidence are also present.71 As Stephen Van
Evera has famously observed, \"At least some false optimism about
relative power preceded every major war since 1740, as well as many
lesser and ancient wars.\" 72 [**Oil-wealthy** states\' excessive
military **spending** can increase the **overconfidence** of their
leaders, who see **expensive** weapons and extensive **military
outlays** and assume they have]{.underline} assembled [a **superior**
fighting **force** capable of achieving **victory** at a **low
cost**.]{.underline}

Ba\'athist Iraq---a particularly belligerent case---provides an excel-
lent illustration of this process. Saddam Hussein\'s conviction in his
own military genius, his faith in new armaments, and his belief that his
troops performed well (despite all evidence) often led him to seek
no-win conflicts.73 His decision to launch a war with Iran in 1980 was
based on a series of misperceptions, assuming his own army was strong
and well armed while his enemy was weak. Hussein had initiated a major
military expansion effort\" during the late 1970s, making his army the
second-largest in the Middle East, provisioned with top-of- the-line
weapons. Yet as Caitlin Talmadge succinctly points out, \"Lack of
material capability was not the primary Iraqi problem in fighting
Iran.\"74 Hussein overestimated the potential performance of his troops
and charged into what he expected would be a quick, easy war; as one
scholar noted, \"Iraq \... has committed the common mistake of trying to
bite off more than it can chew, having overestimated its own power and
underestimated that of its opponent.\"75 Iran was both more powerful
than expected and not at all interested in a limited war, resulting in a
decade-long war.76 Another scholar points out that \"Saddam Hussein
should have had serious misgivings regarding Iraq\'s capacity to sustain
a prolonged and ferocious conflict.\" 77 Swept away by overconfidence in
his well-equipped army, he apparently had no such doubts.

Libya\'s Muammar Gaddafi made similar mistakes. Though he had in many
ways hobbled his army\'s ability to fight with his aggressive
coup-proofing, he continued to purchase arms and equipment. Despite
repeated provocations against his neighbors---including a major
intervention in Uganda to protect dictator Idi Amin---Libya was
victorious in none of these campaigns. In Chad, Libyan troops were
barely able to achieve a stalemate against Chadian forces and were
repeatedly beaten back by French supporting troops.78 Gaddafi also
escalated conflicts against states that far outclassed him. Under his
rule, Libya claimed maritime control over the Gulf of Sirte, challenging
the United States militarily and suffering substantial losses as a
result. As one observer noted, \"Despite the political and military
advantages the United States enjoyed in this forum, Gaddafi repeatedly
took the bait.\" Gaddafi\'s overconfidence was particularly puzzling
given his repeated defeats at the hands of superior LIS forces: \"Having
had his nose bloodied each time, one wonders why Gaddafi believed things
might turn out differently the next time." 79

This offers one potential explanation for the findings in chapter and
goes some way toward explaining why [**[oil-rich]{.mark}** states start
**more wars**: their [**overconfident** leaders **mistake** high
military spending for **actual capability**]{.mark}]{.underline}. It
also suggests an unfortunate corollary: [**oil-wealthy** states are
**not only** more likely to **start wars** but]{.underline} may be more
[likely to **lose them**. [Oil wealth may]{.mark}]{.underline} even
[[**cushion** the]{.mark} negative [**impact** of]{.mark}]{.underline}
losing a [**[war]{.mark}**, providing ways [to **buy
off**]{.mark}]{.underline} segments of [[society and **allowing** a
leader to maintain]{.underline}]{.mark} his hold on [[**power**
despite]{.mark} repeated [conflict]{.mark} **losses**]{.underline}.80
Yet whether oil wealth is indeed a propelling or merely a facilitating
cause of war, it is clear that the possession of large quantities of oil
wealth can have significant military benefits for those leaders with the
wit and willpower to exploit them wisely.

Conclusion

For normal states, there\'s a strong connection between war and wealth.
Leaders\' decisions about military spending are constrained by their
resources, a trade-off most effectively summed up by the simplest of
economic models: guns versus butter. The question of resourcing wars is
a fundamentally political one, with implications for taxation, economic
growth, social spending, and even domestic stability. For oil-rich
states, however, that is not the case. High levels of oil and gas income
allow oil-wealthy states to spend freely on the military, on armaments,
and on military research and development, often without substantial
budgetary trade-offs. The data bears this out: they spend substantially
more than non-petrostates in military expenditure and on armaments. They
are even more likely to have an active nuclear weapons research program.
The notion of resource arms racing---of oil-wealthy states\' driving up
arms sales and fund large militaries---is highly plausible.

As this chapter shows, the ways in which oil-rich states use their oil
wealth for military means varies widely, depending on the
characteristics of the state and the foreign and domestic threats it
faces. Oil-wealthy states buy a variety of weapons from different
suppliers, oriented toward the specific threats each faces. But on
average, across the board, they spend substantially more than their
non-oil-producing counterparts. This spending is also somewhat
inelastic---spending increases when oil revenues rise but does not fall
as quickly, a long-term fiscal concern for policymakers in these states.
Indeed, oil-rich states\' boom-bust social spending is already known to
produce heavy debt spirals; military spending appears to follow a
similar pattern.81 Russia\'s recent military modernization highlights
the perils of this approach. [[The **Kremlin** enjoyed]{.mark} big
[successes]{.mark} in [using **oil wealth** to improve its]{.mark}
ailing **[military]{.mark}**]{.underline} before collapsing oil prices
forced it to make major budgetary cuts to the program. Meanwhile, Libya
highlights another problem faced by wealthy oil states: whether due to
weak institutions, insufficient political will, or a choice to
prioritize coup- proofing, military spending does not necessarily
produce battlefield effectiveness in a military.

But these caveats are in many ways beside the point. The data clearly
shows that oil wealth helps to drive military spending. Indeed,
oil-wealthy states spend so much on arms and armaments that the price of
oil is closely correlated not only to the spending habits of individual
states but to global levels of arms sales. The downsides to this
connection are legion. [Military **[spending]{.mark}** can [drive
**security spirals** and **arms races**]{.mark}]{.underline}. And as
studies have often shown, arms sales are far more often destabilizing
than stabilizing.82 Research suggests that [the **[recent]{.mark}** wave
of **[conflict]{.mark}** that has [consumed the Middle East is
**endogenous** to **high oil prices**]{.mark} during the
2000s]{.underline}.83 Policymakers should be aware that, thanks to
petrostates, [[**high** oil **prices**]{.mark} can]{.underline}
sometimes [[be a **recipe** for]{.mark} regional
**[instability]{.mark}**]{.underline}. We build upon this discussion in
chapter 4, exploring the other uses of oil wealth in foreign policy.
From the dark side (petroleum proxies) to the light side (oil altruism),
the following chapter aims to show that oil wealth impacts foreign
policy through more than just military spending.

#### High prices cause war\-\--satiates opposition at home and boosts war chests. 

**Bunzel 18**, M.A., Global Theory and International Economics, formerly

[[stability of **petrostates**]{.underline}]{.mark}. This makes ample

**palliate**]{.mark} needy [**populations** and]{.mark} potential [elite

There are, however, several complications to this general intuition.
Some states already have sufficiently buoyant revenues relative to their
small populations to satisfy their publics and feed clientelistic
networks. Providing largesse can also backfire if prices drop; taking
away something valuable that people have grown used to is a dangerous
game, especially when elites aren't ready to play it. And then of course
there is the famed "oil curse": For all sorts of reasons, from "Dutch
disease" economic distortions to the derangement of normal citizen-state
relationships, oil riches can in time undermine regimes, weakening and
even destroying them.

**example**]{.underline} of that dynamic.

The fifth reason to [have [**confidence** in petro-**aggression** comes

The country-case histories in Petro-Aggression illustrate the causal
mechanisms. For instance, the cases show that [[oil revenues offer
**petro**]{.mark}**state** [leaders]{.mark}]{.underline} considerable
[political **[autonomy]{.mark}** from their population, [shielding them
from]{.mark} the political [**backlash** if they engage in]{.mark}
military **[adventurism]{.mark}**]{.underline} in foreign policy.
Remarkably, no petro-revolutionary leader who lost a war has been forced
out of office due to domestic political backlash, even though leaders of
non-petrostates who lose wars are frequently punished this way.15
Anticipation of those outcomes should induce leaders in non-petrostates
to be cautious about instigating military conflicts, but
[[leaders]{.mark} in **petrostates** [need not be]{.mark} so
**[cautious]{.mark}**]{.underline}. In addition, there is strong
qualitative evidence that revolutionary [leaders tend to be **risk
tolerant**, which plausibly makes them more **likely** to engage in
interstate **conflict**.]{.underline}

The sixth and final reason to have confidence in petro-aggression stems
from considering the full range of efforts to test petro-aggression's
empirical robustness. Jang and Smith conclude that the evidence for
petro-aggression is "not robust" and "quite weak," phrases which
normally suggest the finding is fragile to multiple alternative
specifications.16 Yet, [in my]{.underline} 2010 article and [2013
book]{.underline} on petro-aggression, I described some of the many
robustness checks I conducted. For instance, [I used data from the
**I**nternational **C**risis **B**ehavior dataset as a **measure** of
conflict instead of **MIDs**]{.underline}. I also used other measures of
the dependent variable, such as the "Side A" indicator for the conflict
initiator. I used four different variants of the definition of
petrostate (for example, continuous measures of oil income per capita or
as fraction of GDP), each of which were substituted for the dichotomous
measure of petrostate, and the regressions retested. [[I used **logit**
and **rare events**]{.mark} logit [regressions]{.mark}]{.underline}. The
dataset on revolutionary governments has a secondary variable called
ambiguous that indicate borderline cases or cases where information was
missing; that variable was used for robustness testing. I used
dichotomous measures of democracy instead of continuous ones. [I
[added]{.mark} **various** potential [**control**
variables]{.mark}]{.underline}, such as: year fixed effects; a lagged
dependent variable ([to control [for]{.mark} **rivalries** and
[**ongoing** disputes]{.mark}]{.underline}); and the Correlates of War
measure composite indicator of national capability (CINC). [The
[**models** were]{.mark} also **[retested]{.mark}**]{.underline} when
the sample was restricted to developing countries only, and when [all
the independent **variables** were **lagged**]{.underline} by one year,
[to avoid possible endogeneity]{.underline}. [[In **none** of
these]{.mark} robustness [**tests** did **any**]{.mark} of the [results
**change**]{.mark}]{.underline} materially.

### I/L\-\--High Prices\-\--Econ\-\--AT: Oil DA\-\--2AC

#### Oil rents are [volatile]{.underline}, [monopolize growth]{.underline} vectors, and cause [inequality]{.underline} and [corruption]{.underline}: kills development. 

**GIM 22**, Generation Investment Management, an ESG-based investment

(GIM, 2022, "Fossil Fuels, the Economy and Instability: Why the world's

https://www.generationim.com/our-thinking/insights/fossil-fuels-the-economy-and-instability-why-the-world-s-dependence-on-fossil-fuels-hurts-the-economy-and-creates-instability/)

Take the economy first. Using World Bank data, we divide all countries
into "oil-dependent" and "non-oil dependent" states, using a simple
threshold: a country is oil-dependent if profits from oil account for
more than 5% of GDP. Our analysis finds that [in the past decade the

considerably **[more slowly]{.mark}**]{.underline} (1.8% a year on
average) [[than **others**]{.underline}]{.mark} (2.6% a year on
average).

These states are also more likely to be "one-track economies", where
[[oil **earnings** are the only]{.mark} true [source]{.mark} of foreign
earnings]{.underline}. In the Republic of the [Congo, for
instance]{.underline}, where [oil profits account for]{.underline}
nearly [**half** of]{.underline} registered [**GDP**, there is **not
much else** to sustain the economy]{.underline}. These countries tend to
be [[subject to **boom-and-bust**]{.mark} cycles [because]{.mark}
commodity [**prices** tend to be]{.mark} quite
**[volatile]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

[Oil-dependent countries **[also]{.mark}** tend to be more
**unequal**]{.underline}. Recent research on Iran, for instance, has
found [[a]{.mark} **compelling [link]{.mark}** [between]{.mark} oil
[**booms** and]{.mark} higher **[inequality]{.mark}**]{.underline}.
Russia has some of the world's highest levels of inequality of income
and wealth.

[Why does a **reliance** on hydrocarbons lead to such poor economic
outcomes? One reason]{.underline} relates to "Dutch disease". This is a
phenomenon whereby [**[high]{.mark}** levels of
**[exports]{.mark}**]{.underline} result in currency appreciation, which
[[ends up making **non-resource** sectors
**uncompetitive**]{.underline}]{.mark} on international markets.
Well-paid jobs in oil and gas draw the smartest people in,
[[depriving]{.mark} other parts of [the **economy**]{.mark}]{.underline}
of their expertise. Oil-and-gas production is a highly monopolistic
industry: only a small number of people control the industry at any one
time, contributing to high inequality.

[Another reason]{.underline} relates to corruption. [[Corrupt
societies]{.mark} tend to [have **weaker**]{.mark} economic
**[growth]{.mark}**]{.underline}. In part this is because weak property
rights dissuade investment; in part this is because getting stuff done
is just more difficult. [A huge range of [**evidence** shows]{.mark}
that **oil-dependent** states tend to be more **corrupt**]{.underline}.
One paper by two IMF researchers, for instance, found that [an "increase
in **[oil rents]{.mark}** significantly [increase]{.mark}s
**[corruption]{.mark}**]{.underline}". This is in large part because the
potential payoffs to corruption --- in the form of access to huge
reserves of liquid gold --- are high.

###  1AR\-\--I/L\-\--High Prices\-\--Small States

#### Oil rents turn [growth]{.underline}, [corruption]{.underline}, [violence]{.underline}, and [instability]{.underline}. Renewables solve. 

**GIM 22**, Generation Investment Management, an ESG-based investment
firm citing World Bank data and academic research on resource curses
(GIM, 2022, "Fossil Fuels, the Economy and Instability: Why the world's
dependence on fossil fuels hurts the economy and creates instability,"
GIM,
https://www.generationim.com/our-thinking/insights/fossil-fuels-the-economy-and-instability-why-the-world-s-dependence-on-fossil-fuels-hurts-the-economy-and-creates-instability/)

[For similar reasons [**oil-rich** states are]{.mark} also **more
likely** to be [politically **unstable**]{.mark}]{.underline}---the
second part of our argument. In part [this is [a **direct result** of
the fact]{.mark} that [their economies are
**weaker**]{.mark}]{.underline}. But [there is something inherently
**unstable** about a country that **relies** heavily on **fossil
fuels**. [Whoever is in charge of]{.mark} **oil [rents]{.mark}**
[has]{.mark} a [licence to **print money**]{.mark}]{.underline}: beyond
maintenance, pumping oil neither requires much particular expertise nor
new ideas. [[**Control** is all that **matters**. As such]{.mark},
**political [violence]{.mark}** [has]{.mark} very **[high
rewards]{.mark}**. "Strongmen" political leaders are **more likely** to
emerge]{.underline}. Might is right.

Fossil-fuel-rich states are, indeed, more likely to be autocratic. A
recent paper in World Politics, an academic journal, found convincing
evidence that "oil exports are strongly associated with authoritarian
rule \[and\] that this effect is not limited to the Middle East".

Generation's analysis of data from Freedom House , a research
organisation, backs up this point. Oil-dependent countries, we estimate,
are approximately 40% less free than other countries (as measured on a
one-to-seven scale). In addition, oil-dependent countries have become
considerably less free over the past 30 years (other countries have
become somewhat more free over the same period). Oil, in sum, is the
enemy of democracy.

CONCLUSION

It is important to acknowledge that [[the West's]{.mark} continued
[**reliance** on oil]{.mark} and gas [perpetuates the]{.mark}
"**resource [curse]{.mark}**"]{.underline}. By using fossil fuels, we
are not simply cooking the planet, as bad as that may be. We are also
[[consigning]{.mark} a huge **chunk** of [the world]{.mark}'s
**population** [to weak]{.mark} economic [growth, **political
violence**]{.mark}]{.underline} and undemocratic government.

There are good reasons to believe that [**[renewable]{.mark}**-energy
**generation**]{.underline}, operated at scale, [[will **not** have
these]{.mark} **same** pernicious **[effects]{.mark}**]{.underline}. For
one, a large body of research suggests that [renewable
energy]{.underline} is more labour-intensive: this [helps **spread** the
economic **rewards** around more widely]{.underline}, reducing
inequality. The geographical distribution of renewable-energy generation
--- both within countries and between them --- is also wider. Few
countries can produce oil; but very few countries cannot produce
renewable energy. [[Most]{.mark} people [cannot dig an **oil well** in
their **garden**, but]{.mark} most people [can put]{.mark} a
**[solar]{.mark} panel** [on their **roof**]{.mark}. The political
dynamics are thus completely **different**.]{.underline}

Russia's invasion of Ukraine has focused minds on the consequences of
Western energy dependence. It is, however, also important to cast the
net wider. [Hydrocarbons]{.underline} are not just environmentally
unsustainable: they [weaken the **social**, political and **economic**
fabric]{.underline} too. We can only hope that this crisis will
accelerate the transition away from fossil fuels and towards a cleaner
future.

#### This is a [consensus]{.underline} in the [literature]{.underline}. 

**Meiners and Morriss 22**, \*Goolsby-Rosenthal Chair in Economics and
Law, University of Texas at Arlington, Ph.D. in Economics from Virgnia
Tech, \*\*Professor, Bush School of Government and Public Service and
the School of Law at Texas A&M University, Ph.D. in Economics from MIT
(\*Roger E. Meiners, \*\*Andrew P. Morriss, 2022, "Addressing Green
Energy's "Resource Curse"," Duke environmental Law and Policy Forum,
Vol. XXXIII, No. 1, University of Kansas Libraries, Hein Online)

Correlation is not, of course, causation. Prof. Røed Larsen of the
Norwegian Business School notes that oil has been good for that
nation.62 There are examples that both illustrate and contradict the
resource curse hypothesis. While [the **DRC**, rich in]{.underline}
diamonds, cobalt and [other resources, has been a **sad**
story]{.underline} for a long time, **[Botswana]{.underline}**, also
diamond rich, has done better than [most resource-rich **African**
nations]{.underline} both economically and with respect to governance.63
Some empirical work has been critical of the resource curse findings,64
including one statistical study finding that resource wealth is
beneficial [in East **Asia** and **Latin America**]{.underline}.65
[Taking [the literature **as a whole**]{.mark}, however, it would be
fair to say that **[more often]{.mark} than not**, **[oil]{.mark}** and
mineral **[wealth]{.mark}** have [been associated with **less** economic
**development**]{.mark}]{.underline} and less personal freedom.

While there is broad agreement that this correlation exists, there is no
consensus explanation for why it exists. Most analyses suggest it is
related to the quality of governance and property rights institutions,
an analysis which has a "chicken-and-egg" flavor to it.66 Other studies
have tried to measure the rule of law, finding that weak institutions
mean a lack of constraints on plundering by the ruling class.67 Again,
this does not show how to resolve the problem. [In many
nations]{.underline}, the generation of [**[wealth]{.mark}** from
resources **[does not]{.mark}** help to [create]{.mark} better
**[institutions]{.mark}**]{.underline}.68 Some analyses suggest that
[natural [**resources** increase]{.mark} costly [**internal struggles**
for control]{.mark}]{.underline}.69 For example, Auty argues that growth
in a country requires reinvestment of rents70 in markets, not
politics.71 [In resource-rich countries, **[rents]{.mark}**]{.underline}
from resources [[are]{.mark} often [**used** in political
contests]{.mark} for **control**]{.underline} and are controlled by the
ruling class, [[rather than]{.mark} being [**invested** in
wealth]{.mark}-creating activities]{.underline} that boost the wider
economy over time.72 Not surprisingly, [resource-[rich
countries]{.mark}]{.underline} often [**[suffer]{.mark}** under
[authoritarian governments]{.mark}]{.underline}73 [[and]{.mark} from
**[internal conflict]{.mark}**]{.underline}.74 [[Economies that relied
on]{.mark} **extraction**]{.underline} and exports [from
[oil]{.mark}]{.underline}, minerals or plantation crops [[were]{.mark}
**more [likely]{.mark}** [to]{.mark}]{.underline} develop slavery,
dictatorships and state control75 and [[suffer]{.mark} from **greater**
levels of **[corruption]{.mark}**]{.underline}.76

**Destek et al. 22**, \*Professor in the Department of Economics at

#### It's [empirically confirmed]{.underline} and causes [devastating corruption]{.underline}. 

**Mohammed 21**, M.A. in Corruption and Governance from The Centre for

The subject of extensive research, [the **resource curse**]{.underline},
or "paradox of plenty", [points to an inverse relationship where
**wealth** brings a **detrimental** impact]{.underline}. Nigeria -- the
largest oil producer in Africa, the sixth-largest global exporter, holds
the tenth-largest proven oil reserve in the world -- is arguably such a
"cursed" nation.

[[Dependent on]{.underline}]{.mark} their [natural resource
**[exports]{.mark}**, these [countries have]{.mark} on average, **[lower
growth]{.mark}** rates, [lower]{.mark}]{.underline} levels of [human
**[development]{.mark}**, and [more **inequality** and poverty]{.mark}.
They [also]{.mark} have]{.underline} been found to have [worse
**institutions** and **[more conflict]{.mark}**]{.underline} than
resource-poor economies.

It arises predominantly due to poor political governance and weak
institutions, coming from the distinct phenomena around oil exploitation
rather than possession -- and is shaped by the multinationals, national
and foreign governments, the foreign financiers and investors, alongside
the structures of states and private actors in oil exporting countries.

[Resource **wealth** can have a **devastating** impact]{.underline}.
Oil-exporting nations such as [[**Nigeria**, **Venezuela**, **Angola**

[[economies **devastated**]{.underline}]{.mark}, but there have been

**indicative** of]{.mark} is **[corruption]{.mark}**]{.underline}: a

to]{.mark} economic and social **[development]{.mark}**, significantly

[**[\$2tn]{.mark}** is [siphoned]{.mark} away **[annually]{.mark}** by

bridge]{.mark} the **[global infrastructure]{.mark}** gap.]{.underline}

As defined by Transparency International, corruption is an "abuse of
entrusted power for personal or private gain." In 1996, the World Bank's
then president James D Wolfensohn called it a cancer and challenged all
countries to strive for transparency and accountability to combat the
pernicious impact of corruption on society, defining the consequences as
redirecting resources from the poor to the wealthy, inflating business
costs, discouraging foreign direct investment (FDI), draining public
expenditure, misdirecting aid and undermining equitable national
development.

#### The risk is high. 

**Ross 8**, Associate Professor of Political Science, UCLA (Michael
Ross, 2008, "Blood Barrels: Why Oil Wealth Fuels Conflict," Foreign
Affairs, Vol. 87, No. 2, University of Kansas Libraries, Hein Online)

OIL ON FIRE

For new oil and gas producers, the gravest danger is the possibility of
armed conflict. Among developing countries, an oil producing country is
twice as likely to suffer internal rebellion as a non-oil-producing one.
The conflicts range in magnitude from low-level [[**secessionist**
struggles]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as those occurring [[in]{.mark} the
**[Niger]{.mark}** Delta and]{.underline} southern [Thailand, [to
**full**]{.mark}-blown **[civil wars]{.mark}**]{.underline}, such as
[[in **Algeria**, Colombia, **Sudan**, and]{.underline}]{.mark}, of
course, **[[Iraq]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

[[Oil **wealth**]{.mark} can [trigger **conflict**]{.mark}]{.underline}
in three ways. First, it can cause [[economic
**instability**]{.underline}]{.mark}, which then [[leads to
**political** instability]{.underline}]{.mark}. When people lose their
jobs, they become more frustrated with their government and more
vulnerable to being recruited by rebel armies that challenge the
cash-starved government. A sudden drop in income can result in internal
strife in any country, but [because **oil prices** are]{.underline}
unusually [volatile, oil-producing **countries** tend to be **battered**
by cycles of]{.underline} [**booms** and **busts**]{.underline}. And the
more dependent a government is on its oil revenues, the more likely it
is to face turmoil when prices go south.

Second, [[**oil wealth** often]{.mark} helps [support
**insurgencies**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Rebellions in many countries fail
when their instigators run out of funds. But raising money in
petroleum-rich countries is relatively easy: [insurgents can steal oil
and **sell it** on the **black market**]{.underline} (as has happened
[in **Iraq** and **Nigeria**]{.underline}), extort money from oil
companies working in remote areas (as in Colombia and Sudan), or find
business partners to fund them in exchange for future consideration in
the event they seize power (as in Equatorial Guinea and the Republic of
the Congo).

Third, [oil wealth encourages **separatism**]{.underline}. Oil and gas
are usually produced in self-contained economic enclaves that yield a
lot of revenue for the central government but provide few jobs for
locals-who also often bear the costs of petroleum development, such as
lost property rights and environmental damage. To reverse the imbalance,
some locals seek autonomy from the central government, as have the
people in the [petroleum-rich **regions** of]{.underline} Bolivia,
Indonesia, [**Iran**, Iraq, **Nigeria**, and **Sudan**]{.underline}.

This is not to say that petroleum is the only source of such conflicts
or that it inevitably breeds violence. In fact, almost half of all the
states that have produced oil since 197o have been conflict-free.
[Oil]{.underline} alone cannot create conflict, but it both
[[exacerbates]{.mark} latent [**tensions** and gives
governments]{.mark}]{.underline} and their [more [**militant**
opponents]{.mark}]{.underline} the means to fight them out. Governments
that limit corruption and put their windfalls to good use rarely face
unrest. Unfortunately, [**oil** production is now rising]{.underline}
precisely [in those **countries** where wise leadership is]{.underline}
often [in **short supply**]{.underline}. Most of the new [energy-rich
states]{.underline} are [in **Africa**]{.underline} (Chad, C6te
d\'lvoire, Mauritania, Namibia, and Sao Tom6 and Principe), [the
**Caspian** basin]{.underline} (Azerbaijan, Kazakhstan, and
Turkmenistan), [or Southeast **Asia**]{.underline} (Cambodia, East
Timor, Myanmar, and Vietnam). Almost [all are undemocratic]{.underline}.
The majority are [very **poor** and ill equipped to manage]{.underline}
a sudden and [large **influx** of **revenues**]{.underline}. And many
also have limited petroleum reserves-just enough to yield large revenues
for a decade or two-which means that if they succumb to civil war, they
will squander whatever chance they had of using their oil windfalls to
escape from poverty.

### I/L\-\--High Prices\-\--Green Paradox\-\--AT: Oil DA\-\--2AC

#### Green paradox wrong\-\--companies can't just up and immediately drill more oil and increases are offset\-\--best model.

**Cairns and Smith 19**, \*Department of Economics and Cireq, McGill
University, \*\*Edwin L. Cox School of Business, Southern Methodist
University (\*Robert D. Cairns, \*\*James L. Smith, 2019, "The Green
Paradox, A Hotelling Cul de Sac," Economics of Energy and Environmental
Policy, Vol. 8, No. 2, University of Kansas Libraries, JSTOR)

5\. CONCLUSION

The researcher's dilemma is that, to construct a general equilibrium
model with an endogenous price, one has to abstract from a fundamental
property of production and hence of that equilibrium. We have chosen to
retain the fundamental property and to wring as much as possible from a
partial equilibrium analysis. Our model shifts attention from temporally
smooth, convex decisions about the flow of aggregate production to
lumpy, irreversible decisions that create specific, persisting limits to
flows from distinct reserves. Extraction is perceived as an industrial
rather than a consumptive activity. [[Producers **cannot**
increase]{.mark} production [**at will**. They produce at]{.mark} a
[capacity]{.mark}]{.underline} that is [[**predetermined** by]{.mark}
**sunk** [investment]{.mark}]{.underline} and diminished by natural
decline. [Any **increase** in production **requires** sinking a
**further** investment. The]{.underline} incremental discounted [net
**cash flows** must pay back that **investment**]{.underline}. The
analytically important notion is net present value, not the rate of cash
flow: Decisions are made on several margins, but not on the basis of the
ratios of net cash flows.

The single reserve is the locus of decisions for the production of oil.
Each reserve has its own level of development and decline parameter.
Whatever its tilt, a unit tax has qualitatively strong effects, namely,
lower investment and initial production, reduced net present value,
delays and reductions in enhanced recovery, and, in most cases, reduced
discounted carbon damages and reduced overall recovery. [[**Contrary**
to]{.mark} the]{.underline} underlying vision of the [**[green
paradox]{.mark}**, the]{.underline} "tilt" of [**[output]{.mark}** from
an oil reservoir]{.underline} (and hence in the aggregate) [[is
**equal** to the rate of]{.mark} natural **[decline]{.mark}** of the
reserve]{.underline}, which depends on the tax only through the initial
investment.

The incentive to reduce initial investment is stronger for a decreasing
tax than an increasing tax. On the other hand, an increasing tax
discourages investment in enhanced production, thereby further reducing
the total level of output from the reserve as well as the length of
production. Dynamic arbitrage is clear in the results for enhanced
production and to an extent for the announcement effect. Stressed here
is the choice of capacity, because it determines the level of output and
because of the predictions of the green paradox.

The aggregation of decisions and the endogenous equilibrium price path
in Hotelling analyses result from oversimplification. In the study of
the green paradox, questions of comparability among reserves and of
levels of tax effort for different tilts of the tax and different
qualities of reserve are neglected. [In [our
**model**]{.mark}]{.underline} the putative sectoral equilibrium
[[involves]{.underline}]{.mark} the outcomes of [decisions at **tens of
[thousands]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}]{.underline} heterogeneous developed
and undeveloped **[[reserves]{.underline}]{.mark}** and undiscovered
resources. There is in fact no set of parameters among the many
background variables that can be held fixed in order to make the
scenarios comparable. [Even **[though]{.mark}** the [firms are]{.mark}
considered to be **[price takers]{.mark}**]{.underline}, simulating the
equilibrium would involve heroic assumptions about policy making and
about the physical and economic characteristics of undiscovered
reserves. Common **[[assumptions]{.underline}]{.mark}**, such as
[[that]{.mark} marginal [**costs** are equalized]{.mark} across
**reserves**]{.underline} or that all discovered reserves are in
production, [[do **not hold**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Discerning sectoral
effects, including market prices and total emissions, is mathematically
untenable and requires general economic reasoning.

There is a large literature on optimal resource taxation in Hotelling
models. An optimal tax can be imagined for an industry composed of
distinct reserves, as a welfare maximization of the full equilibrium.
The exercise would involve decisions at all properties, undiscovered,
undeveloped, being developed and developed. An optimal tax is not a
carbon tax, which aims to reduce carbon emissions and damages.

We have considered the class of dynamic, per-unit taxes on carbon
emissions, and consequently on oil output, as is assumed in work on the
paradox and is being implemented in a number of jurisdictions. We have
found a strong tendency for such taxes, be they increasing, decreasing
or constant, to reduce investment and output and hence early (and later)
emissions. Resource consequences can contrast with environmental
consequences, [however: **deadweight** losses can generate **costs**
that substantially **exceed** the **benefits**.]{.underline}

There is one powerful, incontrovertible sectoral effect:
[[unlike]{.underline}]{.mark} in Hotelling models of [the [**green
paradox**, which predict **increases** in **output**]{.mark} at
producing reserves]{.underline}, in the face of the introduction of a
tax [[there is **no change** in]{.mark} current **[production]{.mark}**
from active reserves. For]{.underline} predictions of [the **paradox**
to be realized]{.underline}, the depressing effect of the tax on
investments in development, as well as [the natural
**decline**]{.underline} at producing reserves, [would have to be
**overcome** through hastened **development**]{.underline} of known but
undeveloped reserves. [Ultimate **[production]{.mark}** from each
**reserve** [would be **unambiguously lower**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

It is vital to begin with a meaningful representation of reality. Our
results for interacting decisions on several margins indicate what needs
to be explained about effects of carbon taxes on emissions. The
[simulations suggest]{.underline} that [the **green paradox** is **not**
a **possibility**]{.underline} of policy interest. As in any model, the
results are no better than the underlying assumptions. Our
simplifications suggest at the least that [the **industry** is far
**more complicated**]{.underline} and subtler [than]{.underline} a
Hotelling [analysis can represent]{.underline} and that such analysis
should not be used for policy. A pertinent objection to a tax is that
deadweight losses, unobserved in conventional analyses, may render it a
socially costly way to reduce carbon emissions. The best bet for society
may be a rising tax, in terms both of the social efficiency in the use
of a scarce resource and of reasonably accounting for the negative
effects of carbon emissions on the environment.
